If you see a corroding sore in a place that can tolerate a cautery with fire, then prepare many cauteries, small and large, according to what is suitable for the place where the corroding sore is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and none of it remains in any way.
Then touch the same for three days, and apply over the cauterized place burnt sulfur with oil, until all eschars are eradicated, and all corruption.
Then heal it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days sound flesh is born without corruption, it is good.
And if not: then repeat the cautery over that which remains of the corrupted places.
And the corroding is treated whenever with sharp medicine. It itself rises in place of the cautery.
But indeed the cautery with fire is of quicker relief: and I have already spoken of its cauterization with sharp medicine in division: therefore take it there when you need it.
On the method of cauterizing in hammer-like tumors and warts.
Chapter 55.
It often happens that this sickness occurs in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a coarse, foul thing which harms the foot.
And indeed the cautery for them is in two ways: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
But as for the one with fire, it is like a hollow cautery of a falcon's feather, which is made from iron, according to the quantity that the hammer contains on all sides: and let its edges be subtle.
Then apply it ignited upon the hammer-like tumor. Then turn the hand with the cautery around the hammer-like tumor, until the cautery penetrates to the depth of the tumor: and leave it for three days, until it begins to discharge pus. Then plaster it with crushed forest herbs, ground with salt: and leave the plaster on it during the night. For it will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the place with a generating ointment of flesh until it heals.
What if they are not turned and often occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then administer to them cauterization with hot water: and it is, that you take a small tube made of bronze, or iron, or from a feather of a vulture: and put it on the nail, or the ulcer. Then throw into the tube a small quantity of hot water: hold your hand, and you turn it with compression of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the tube is impressed on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the weak one wait for an hour on the biting of the hot water.
Then release it, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you come to all of them that are on the body.
Then treat them after they have been eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
On the method of cauterizing for Tremor.
When it happens to someone that he has a Tremor from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauterizations, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between all the vertebrae a cauterization, and on his chest a cauterization, and on his stomach a cauterization.
For the Tremor is stilled with an oily cauterization, and health hastens to cold feverish illness.
On the method of cauterizing for Albaithar ( possibly a misspelling or variation of "Alb
Chapter 57.
When foul Albathar sores are expelled from the body, that is, pustules, which are made from corrupted gross matter.
It is necessary therefore in the beginning when they appear, that the pustules on the entire head should be cauterized with a subtle cauterization of myrtle wood, whose extremities have been kindled with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lentil-shaped cauterizer.
And when they are cauterized with an adamant, at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method: and it does not increase: and the superfluity is disrupted making them, and they are healed from it.
Truly however it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
It often happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut at the site of a wound, which occurs externally, or at the site of an incision of a postule, or cauterization of a limb, and similar things to these: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When therefore it happens to anyone, then apply your hand quickly to the orifice of the artery, and put your index finger on top of it: and press it strongly, until the blood is blocked under your finger, and does not come out from it.
Then put into the fire small olive cauterizers, and a large number of them: and blow on them until they are ignited strongly.
Then take one of them, small or large, according to the wound and the place in which the artery is disrupted, and put the cauterizer on the vein itself, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cauterizer until the blood is stopped.
If the blood is expelled when you lift your finger from the orifice of the artery, and you extinguish the cauterizer, then quickly take another cauterizer from the ones that have been prepared in the fire.
You should not stop doing that with one thing after another until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you confuse the nerve that is there, and the injury affects the weak place.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or let it be bound with a strong thread of ligation. Or let medicines of the proper kind be placed on it, which are to cut off blood, and let it be bound with a dressing of suitable constriction.
He who administers the cutting off of it with a ligature, and binding with a cloth, or application of burning things, and similar, does not penetrate to it at all, except rarely.
If it happens to anyone, and there is not a doctor present to him, nor medicine: then let him begin with the placement of the index finger on the opening of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and constriction of it strongly, until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And let the finger not be removed from upon it, with water very cold and violently cold, until the blood congeals, and coagulates, and is cut off.
And meanwhile let him consider what it needs from a cautery or from medicine.
Of the Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in wounds, and similar things.
We have discussed in the first chapter, every kind of illness for which it is suitable to use a cauterizing fire and burning medicine, and its occasions, and its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauterizations.
And I have placed chapters from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter by the same method: that the seeker may seek what is sought regarding it.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, O sons, what in this chapter is an addition to what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of this, it is necessary that there be a more vehement caution, since in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an apostema, or opening of an exit, or cure of an ulcer, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which are associated trembling, and fear, and death often occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest it fall upon you that in which there is hesitation, for sometimes men of a certain type fall into your hands in this art with types of weaknesses. For there is one who becomes angry with his own illness, and death is light to him: on account of the vehemence of what he finds from his weakness and the length of his suffering. And the illness is from fear that signifies death.
And there is one who gives his trust to you, and commits himself to you, hoping for health, and his illness is perilous.
Therefore it is not necessary that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more vehement than your greed and your rapacity. And do not assume anything about it, unless after certain knowledge, which proves to you that which leads to successful treatment.
And may you be skilled in the cure of all your patients, in prognosis and indication of that to which health is most conveniently suited.
For in it is your aid for acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His intellect, and may He not withhold from you guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cauterization, from the neck to the feet: so that may be alleviated for you what you wish from it, and what you need.
On the cure of water that collects in the heads of children.
Chapter One.
Know that this weakness often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife pulls the head of the child with force.
And it also happens sometimes for a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have indeed not seen this sickness in others except in children: and for every one of them whom I saw, death was imminent: therefore it seemed to me that it was advisable to omit the operation in it.
And I have indeed seen one of them, a child, whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head: and the wetness increased until he died.
And this wetness indeed either collects between the skin and the bone: or collects under the bone above the brain.
And the operation on it, if the wetness is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make a single incision in the middle of the head in width: and let the length of the incision be about two knots, until the wetness flows out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If therefore the humidity is greatly increased, and the aposteme is larger, then put two fissures, according to this form.
And if the humidity is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the skull open on every side: and water is pressed out when you press it with your hand to the interior, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you find in the middle of the skull three fissures, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract the whole humidity. Then bind the fissures with bandages and poultices. Then anoint it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then undo the bandage, and cure the wound with dressings and ointments.
And do not cease to bind the head with equality. And feed the weak person with every dry food of little humidity, until the member is comforted and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where appears the greatness of the aposteme, and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or on one side, or to the right, or to the left.
Go up therefore with the incision where it appears to you that the aposteme and the collection of water is: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut the artery, and there occurs a flow of blood, and the weak person dies from that flow with an abundance of humidity.
Of the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter Two.
When there occurs to someone a catarrh to the eyes, or to the breast, and it aggravates that, and the cure of medicines does not help: then the greatest cure is in that, the incision of these two arteries.
It is necessary therefore when one makes an incision on them, that the weak head is shaved. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the weak one constrict his neck, with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places which are behind the ears and depressed: and rarely is it hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a branding iron above both: and make an incision on both sides with a sharp spatula, the incision extending to the mouth: and let it be as wide as the head.
And if necessary, insert the spatula under the artery, and make an incision upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined.
For when the vein is cut, blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which jumps to the interior with a frequent jump.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the quantity of a space of three fingers. Then mark with a branding iron, and extend to the mouth.
And what is necessary to emit from the blood, is six ounces and perhaps you emit more or less from it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the virtue of the weak one and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the ulcer. Then if anything remains at the mouth of the abscess, then cut it, so that a warm aposteme does not occur: then constrict the ulcer with ligatures from a linen cloth.
Then treat it with an ointment, until it is healed.
Of the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter 3.
When an old headache occurs to a man, or sharp catarrh due to acrimonious humidities and heat in the temporal arteries, or old abscesses, and ulcers, and similar things: and it is treated with modes of curative medicine, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these ailments in the extraction of the arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we will narrate.
And the method of operation in extracting them, is that the weak [patient] should shave the hairs which are on the temples, and direct [the instrument] to the apparent artery in the temple. It itself shows to you from its own pulse, and is rarely hidden except in few men, or due to cold withering.
What if it is hidden from you, then the weak [person] should bind his head with any bandage of his: then rub the place with a coarse cloth. Or fumigate the place with hot water, until it appears to you with a clear appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little before. Then scratch the skin with it with ease, until you reach the artery. Then throw into it a hook, and pull it upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and make it protrude from the tunics which are under it on every side.
If the artery is slender, then entwine it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the quantity by which the extremities of it lengthen and contract, and not cause a flow of blood. For if it is not cut and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend inwardly.
Then let out from the blood a sixth of an ounce, up to three.
What if the artery is large then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a double thread of silk or of a string from a bow, so that putrefaction does not reach it before consolidation of the wound and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then cut the surplus which is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour or after another hour.
And if you do, cauterize it with a cauterizing iron up to the mouth, with a knife-shaped cauterizer, until its ends are cut and it stands in the place of the same operation.
And it is a better work, unless he who has it is weak with fever, or is of a hot temperament. For the cauterizer is one of those things which help to end moistures. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with old cotton, and that powders well made be put on top of it.
And after the dressing, let it be treated with dry medicines which make flesh grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
What if meanwhile while you are operating there occurs a flow of blood from the artery, then hasten to cut it with a cauterizer, or so that you fill the place with cotton, and constrict your hand until the bleeding is stopped. What if none of those things are present to you, then put your finger over the place, until the blood coagulates. And moisten the place with water of vehement cold, until the acute pain is stilled, and constricted according to what is necessary.
And of that which is lighter and easier for the extraction of the artery, it is to cauterize the vein with this cauterizer having two knives, and let the sharpness be similar to a double-edged knife. The needle threads are of much smaller thickness than two hairs. For if they are sharp, like two hairs, they will penetrate on both sides and not enter the flesh with speed. When therefore there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: therefore this work is better than any work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space that is between two hairs be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with caustic, and apply both ignited cauteries strongly, until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between the extremities may be prolonged. For they do not consolidate with this cautery deeply.
Chapter 4.
On the treatment of the courses of tears with frequent cauterization from the eyes.
When there are frequent tears and their course is from the veins which are on the outer surface of the skull above the head, and no cure is effective for them with medicines, and you see the face of the infirm already red, and he feels a walking in his forehead like the walking of ants: and his eyes are weak, humid, so that already the hairs of them are corroded by corrosion: and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of tears: then the ancients required for their cure this operation, and they named it cure with Ense.
And it is that you should instruct the infirm to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions in the forehead according to its length: and let the length of the incision be about two fingers, and one incision be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the eyebrows, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are in both sides: and extend your hand from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all incisions be the quantity of three joined fingers: and have a piece of prepared sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula of two acute extremities, whose following shape is a form. From the incision which is on the temporal side, up to the second incision: and excavate with it the whole skin, which is between the two incisions, with the sifac, which is above the mouth. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this spatula, and introduce into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp cultellus, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And put its sharp side towards the flesh joined with the skin, and its blunt side towards the mouth: and drive it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed out equally, press out the places from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then put in all the fissure a dressing from old cloth, and put over them small pieces soaked in wine, and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot aposteme does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the ligature, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with the ointment of royal unguent, and other things which are used to treat wounds: until it heals.
Chapter Five.
Of the treatment of sharp and flowing discharges from the eyes due to internal conditions of the head.
When sharp, acid, and frequent discharges occur to someone, and you see his eyes weakened, pale, and their appearance already debilitated: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and in the depths of the head there is a sharp, laeding pain, and consequent sneezing: then consider among these accidents, that those matters and discharges do not come, unless from many veins.
And better treatment in them is this. And it is, that you order the weak to scratch their own forehead. Then cut in it one fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another a little through its width, and start with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the cutting be up to the bone: and stretch out your hand from the muscles of the temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is laid bare: and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with a cloth of old cloth, or with a linen thread. Then bind above with pads: and soak the pads with wine and oil, so that hot aposteme does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and you see that the hot aposteme has already begun to diminish: then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to be born in it.
Then treat it with a regimen of excitation that makes flesh be born: as if you take two parts from wheat flour, and four parts from colophony: and prepare from them an plaster, and administer to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Of the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the Ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass: or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans: or it is a fluxing thing, such as water and vinegar, and their similars: or some animal.
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from those which do not adhere, nor grow in the ear, expose the Ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then distill into the Ear something from violet oil, or sesame. Then administer the extraction of it, and make him sneeze with condisi, and stop up his nostrils at the approach of sneezing, after you put in the opening of the ear pieces of cloth, or wool, and stretch the ear upwards.
For many times it comes out with this treatment.
If it does not come out, then administer the extraction of it with Gest, that is, finely ground Pizigarolis: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with Gest, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a fine hook, subtly, for a short time.
If therefore it does not come out by that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear deeply, and stop up what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no passage except through the tube: then extract with your breath strong attraction.
For it comes out many times with what we have described. If it does not come out in this manner: then take from helkalimbat, or from glue, that is, prepared gluten, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and put it at the end of the staff after you have wrapped around it a cloth decently placed: then insert it into the opening of the ear with ease, after you have cleansed the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then proceed to cutting, before there occurs a hot abscess, or spasms.
And the method of cutting is, as in phlebotomy, to make an incision in the head at first, and to extract from him blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in the place pressed upon it: and let the incision be of a crescent shape, until you reach the stone: then remove it with that which is possible for you from instruments.
Then you should sew up the wound with speed and care it until it heals.
What if a thing falls into the ear is from one of the grains, which grow and become inflamed: then administer the extraction of it.
If however it does not pass out for you, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the cutting off of that species of grains which fall into the ear and do not do that when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapour of the ear until you put many a poultice: then extract them with forceps or with subtle instruments or with suction, as we have said, for the extraction of it will relieve.
In the water which enters into the ear, it is necessary that he uses a weak snorting with condiments at first: and now has filled his ear with some old cot: and it lies on that ear, in which there is water. If therefore it comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, gentle, and warm them with fire for a little while, and gently put one of them into the opening of his ear: and let him hop on one foot and strike with another stone on the stone which is in the ear. He should not therefore cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out thus, that you take from a pomegranate or from a juniper one, and put one end of it into the ear: and burn the other end with fire until most of it is consumed. Then take another juniper and do that many times until all the water comes out: and extract it with a tube according to what was previously said regarding the stone.
In the extraction of an animal that enters into it, if it is small in body, such as a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I have said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the sense, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all those that are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment is not effective: then it is necessary that you examine the ear in the sun. If, therefore, something appears there of worms, then extract it with forceps, or with subtle hooks.
If however something does not appear there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the subtle extremity into the ear according to the quantity that the weak part tolerates: then suck with a strong suction. Do it many times, until all the worms come out.
If however they do not obey you to come out, then block the surroundings of the cannula with wax, as I have said concerning the stone.
What if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the infection of worms: and you will find it in the treatment of distillations.
And let your pouring be what you pour oil and medicine into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver, or of brass, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let it be therefore that the thing which is in the concavity of the cannula, is made from brass, turned on a lathe. And if you use it, take a rod, and insert cotton into the extremity of its insertion decently: then inject oil, or juice, or what you wish of these medicines into the cannula: and let it be in the ear: then insert the rod with cotton from above, and express it by your hand with tempered compression, until the oil is poured into the concavity of the hearing, and the weak part feels it inside: and that which is poured into the ear, should already be warmed with fire for a little while: and beware lest there be something which distills in it cold moisture: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of an injury to the ear.
Sometimes infants are born from their mothers' wombs not pierced.
And it has already happened to some men that an oblation occurred in the middle of the ear from a wound or flesh born in it.
And this oblation is sometimes made in the depth of the ear's opening, which sight does not reach: and sometimes it happens at the outer opening, and sight reaches it.
The one that happens in the depth of the ear, and which the gaze does not comprehend, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in the one that sight reaches, you should place the ear of the infirm person in the sun and look into it: then if you see an apparent oblation, open it with a subtle spatulum, whose form is this which follows, let its extremity be where there is a small width, of which a certain part is pointed: and the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not harm the ear.
If therefore the oblation is from flesh born in the middle of the ear: then hold it with a subtle hook, and cut it with a vehement facility, until all the flesh is removed.
What if the oblation is in the depth of the ear: then take a subtle and smooth stick, warm it with fire for a little while: then put it into the opening of the ear.
If therefore you perceive the oblation with that which the infirm person finds with heaviness of hearing, prepare its opening with facility: and beware, lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then put on the ear a poultice according to the quantity of the opening's width, which you wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do that for some days, until you are secure about the consolidation of the wound.
Or take cotton cloth and soak it in rose water and sprinkle powdered tragacanth over it.
If you see the abscess has already formed a postule: then it is necessary to extract the cotton cloth and let it out with another cloth which you will have wrapped in cerate made with rose oil until the hot aposteme subsides: then treat it until it heals.
If there is a flow of blood: then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and apply it to the ear: and use the rest of the treatment that stops the flow of blood as described in the treatise on distillations.
Of the treatment for ulcers of the eyelids.
Chapter 8.
These are the ulcers that occur on the eyelids of the eye, when they are moist and when they are dry.
It is necessary therefore to pull them with forceps or with a hook, and to cut them with scissors from their roots. Then apply powdered tragacanth over the place.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a sharp pointed cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Of the treatment for pimples on the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter 9.
It often happens on the eyelids something that resembles a pimple in its thickness and hardness, and because of this it is called a Grando: and it is an aggregation of thick mucus on the upper and lower eyelid.
The operation for them is therefore to examine if it is a pimple proceeding from that which appears on the eyelid and moves to every part, and its treatment is easy.
Find then an incision above it in width. Then scratch it on every side with ease, until it is clean. Then hang it with a hook, and cut it off.
If however the cutting away of it is not prepared for you, unless after you have penetrated the eyelid with a small incision, then it harms the weak one in something.
What if the incision is large: then bring it together with a suture: and treat it, until it heals.
And if it is small, no evil will come to you from it. For an ointment restores it, and fleshes it over.
And if a gland is turned to the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid, and hang the gland with a hook: besides which you will need an incision, and press it on every side.
What if the eyelid is lengthened by an incision, it does not harm it in anything.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the gland, with salt water: and treat the place with that which fleshes it over, until the weak one is healed.
On the treatment of eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a tumor which is generated in the tissues of the upper eyelid: and it happens to children mostly: and it presses on their eyes, and they get cataracts in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears flow to them, and on account of that you see them constantly wiping their faces, and they sleep with them, and their eyelids are humid under their eyebrows, which inflammation has already covered: and a visible sign of Axirnath is an elevation to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the weak person places their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare on it ligatures, and make from that ligature a circle according to the quantity that the Axirnath contains on all sides. Then put it over it, and press with your fingers from all sides, so that the Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that humidity with a spatula making an incision through the width, and let not be an incision greater than what is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut the skin entirely, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many dispositions the Axirnath proceeds from an incision according to the shape of a piece of white fat.
Extract it therefore with a cloth, which you had inserted between your fingers to the exterior, and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed: and beware, lest you put in the incision, so that you do not reach with the spatula to the eye, and harm it.
If however the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should put a little into the incision with ease, until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and put them on the place, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who bruise salt, and put it in the concavity of the incision, so that it melts what remains of that humidity.
Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess forms in the place, then treat it with cooling plasters, until it is healed.
Of the modes of weakness of the eye, that is, when the eyelids become soft, whence it happens that it protrudes as it should not.
Chapter XI
When additional hairs are born in the eyelids of the eye beyond the normal hair growth, and they become long: then they harm the eye, and cause various ailments, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and a white spot, until it becomes a cause for destruction of the eye.
And Ataxmir said that the cure of the eye is by four methods. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, as has been mentioned previously in the chapter on cauterization. Or Ataxmir cures by incision and suture. Or with a needle, as I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the weak one on your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is thus turned back, it is good. And if not: insert the needle into it, where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, where the hairs are born: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the thread.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid under the additional hairs with the spatula incident from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct.
Then extract the thread, and put under the eyelid a small dust from cotton, or wool: then mark on top of the eyelid with ink, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Truly it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid: because it is already differentiated among men.
For there are some in whom it is necessary that a large quantity be cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines that you marked, and start from the larger tear duct up to the smaller tear duct, and let there be a single incision near the natural hairs, as is the thickness of the radius.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scratch out the whole thing. Then bring together with a suture the two lips, with a needle and thread of fine wool: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what overflows from the threads above the two eyebrows, with certain things that irritate, if you want to do that: and if not, do not trouble.
Then leave the suture and threads in place for three days, or four: afterwards attend to it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and tightens. For the eyelid is elevated at the time of the wound's sigillation and its closure: but the future one will be better.
This method of healing was taught by the ancient ones of Ataxmir.
But truly in it is solicitude for the weak, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method exists in Ataxmir also: and it is to mark on the eyelid a figure that is like the shape of the myrtle leaf, as we have described.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this shape that follows, then cut away what overflows with small forceps, according to this shape, with equality of incisions.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not suffice: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and bring together between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then elevate your hand to an equal elevation and elevate the skin over which the knot is marked, as we have described.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and cure it, until it heals.
If a tumor or an aposteme occurs at your incision or your fissure: but seat that tumor with a plaster, and similar things, from sitting ointments.
And when the eyelids also happen to convert their hairs.
It is necessary therefore that you use what we have said in the incision: but the suture of both is more artificial. On the treatment of ataxmy with dogs.
And it is, that you convert the eyelid, and split the fissure which is within, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two rods, or two thin sticks: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and the width of them is less than the width of the spatula: and you have already made an incision at the extremities of both from both parts where you hold the threads.
Then bring together both with subtlety over that which exceeds from the eyelid of the eye, and tie the two rods from both parts with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days. For the constricted skin will die, and become black, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then cure it, until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is elevated: and the hairs do not puncture.
And this is the form of the two rods, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter 12.
On the elevation of puncturing hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation on it is to take a thin needle and insert into it a thread of fine silk, thinly. Then you will gather its extremities, and tie each node with a subtle knot: and the length of the threads is one palm.
Then compose on the Alinxotati another subtle thread below it briefly, and tie its extremities if you wish.
Then the weak one will place his head in your lap, and it should be near the sun as it declares the operation: since it is a subtle operation, or a minute one.
Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you extract from above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up to the thread of the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that hair: if it is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair goes out with the Alinxotati in the eyelid with the natural hair.
If the Alinxotati is pulled and the hair does not go out with it: pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you composed in it, until the Alinxotati goes out from the upper opening, and you see it with the sight of the eye: then you pull the Alinxotati, and the thread: and extract both, and close the eye: and keep it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh is born above it.
If the hair is short, then either associate with it a long hair from the natural hairs, and raise it with that: or leave it until it grows out after a space, and then raise it.
Chapter 13.
When this affliction occurs to the eye, it is called Leporina by Alxatrati.
And this Alxatrati is either a natural or an accidental one.
An accidental one is made from the consolidation of a wound, or from a cut, or from a cauterization, and similar things to these.
And the method of treatment for it is that you find that consolidation and that you separate its lips and that you put between them a linen pad, and bind it, until it heals.
And it is not fitting to use in the treatment of it things which dry and constrict, for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was; but rather use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek: and apply with water, in which the althea has been cooked, linseed: and anoint with dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil: and wrap with that linen, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all ingenuity, that it not consolidate, according to the shape which it was initially.
And this operation which we have spoken of, is not but a certain correction of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return according to what it was entirely.
On the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkle or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter 14.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is what is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one is made from a wound, or a tear, or a cauterization, and similar things to these.
And the method of operation for it is that you take a needle, on which there is double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread passes through both extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with a needle, and cut it with a spatula on the side. If then the figure of the eyelid returns according to what is proper, it is good.
And if not, take a rasp, and put it in the cleft, and convert the eyelid with it: and find two clefts on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two clefts be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is formed from them: until when they are gathered this figure is formed, and it is the letter lauda in Greek.
Then remove that flesh to the amount which is its sharp lower side from that which follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts, and sew it with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we have said, with softening medicines and bandages, until they heal.
And if a crust forms from the cleft and suture or burn, then it is necessary that you find a flat cleft under the hair of the eyelids, also according to what has happened previously. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the discussion on the treatment of Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that it runs in this operation, according to what is prepared from the form of Alxatrati.
It itself is of many diversities in form: and the prudent artificer prepares his skill with whatever method is possible for him, so that he makes it return to the natural figure, or near it, and the infirmity itself, according to what is convenient for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the eye with the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many men, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the whiteness of the eye, and it prevents the same from its natural operations.
Therefore you should insert the extremity of the spatula under the eyelid and raise it up, or stretch it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a subtle spatula, which is not sharp at the point of the incision, but rather dull for a short time: so that the weak one is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and cuts the eye with its sharpness: and your incision should be as if you excise the adhesion with facility, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Pour then into the eye salty water, with water to wash it: or instill into it from a syringe made thus, from dissolved lye with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a linen thread: and put over the eye a woolen cloth soaked in the whiteness of the eye: and use it for three days with dressings, until it is healed.
On the incision of the nail and the elevation of the flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter 16.
Know that the nail is in two ways. Or it is nerveless: and is similar to a subtle, hard callus. Or it is not nerveless, similar to the humidity of frozen white matter: which when it touches iron, or a hook tries to seize it, it is cut off, and the hook does not stick to it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct, until which it goes gradually, until it covers the use, and prevents the light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on it is: that the weak person should put his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with the pincers of a pair of forceps, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert in it a horse hair or an ox hair, or a cow hair: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a little while, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the thread the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were to sew it with the thread up to its posterior.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a subtle spatula, or with small forceps: and remove the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that it does not happen from the cutting that there is a frequent flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail is extended with pincers alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then let it be scraped with a gentle spatula, of which this is the shape.
And beware, lest the spatula touch the cornial tunic, and there happen to it a rupture, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, cast into the eye something of ground salt, or made from zinc oxide, and leave it until another day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
For if the nail is not nerotic, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a pincer be fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and healed as I have described in the division.
For if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the weak person, and take a subtle, gentle, and fine-pointed spatula, according to this shape. And scratch the nail from above with subtle scraping. What if you see that it itself becomes rarefied and broken, and the scraping impresses on it, then distill from your hour on the eye of what has been done with lye or with red or with rough salt: and cover the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation over it, until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye to the contrary: then cease that, and cure the abscess, until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation over it with scraping, until it is healed.
The flesh of the tear duct, if it harms the eye with great annoyance, should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it cut away: and let not the cutting be ultimate, so that the flow of tears is not interrupted.
Then pour into the tear duct either red orpiment, or made from lime, until it is healed.
On the incision of the cataract, and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter 17.
There is born in certain eyes of men a red flesh, composed or overlaid, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps converts the eyelids to the outside: and it is like the rose of the pomegranate.
And the operation for it is also that the weak one places his head on your lap. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks converging to it: and hold it with one forceps or tongs.
Then cut the flesh first, until the whole of it is finished with the incision. And beware of the eye, so that you do not harm it during the operation: and let your incision be what you cut with, or with the spatula, which we described in the incision of the nail: or with small forceps, with which the hazel is collected, according to the form it will take after this chapter.
When the wound is completely healed and all the alguardenici has been removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt or distill into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines, and put on top of the eye a poultice made with egg white.
If some alguardenici remains and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot aposteme: then leave the eye, and cure it with what settles the aposteme.
Then repeat over it with the operation until it is healed.
And do likewise for the flesh that grows on the eye from this condition.
However, it is necessary that you should omit the cure of the whole weak eye from natural or accidental cause, by operating with iron: and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
On the collection of pus from the eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus is a red vein that forms over the eye, and prevents the sight from performing its function: and weakens the eye with the passage of time.
It is necessary therefore for you first to consider if the eye in which there is pus, is strong and does not have any sickness except for the pus: then collect its pus.
And it is that you should instruct the weak person to put their head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your wisdom: and let the hook be subtle for duplication, according to this shape, or let there be two hooks graduated on one body, according to this shape.
Then collect those veins with subtle forceps with subtlety, and remove the blood hour after hour, until you see that the veins are removed from the eye, and dissolved: and beware lest you damage the eye with the extremities of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and you should be firm in your operation indeed: so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have excavated, then pour into the eye a green or red liquid: so that its acidity eats away what remains of the cataract.
If however it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for some days, until the pain is soothed: and it is secured from a hot abscess: then repeat over it the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Of the reduction of the nose to its proper shape.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call it Arissat's fistula, which you will treat with a cautery, or with a medicine of burning acid, according to what its description has previously stated.
And if it does not heal, then there is no ingenuity in it: unless you find a collection of pus above the abscess at its birth, and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see in it a corruption or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called a head scraper.
Let it be made from Indian iron, and let its head be round like a grindstone, which is dotted with the dotting of a file, or the dotting of a fine awl: and place it over the corrupted place of the bone.
Then turn it between your fingers, and you press your hand for a little while, until you know that the corruption has already been scraped: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with medicines of contracting and astringent qualities.
If the place is consolidated, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of blood is stopped: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and is not complicated, and no accident occurs in it: then you should know that it has already healed.
And if not, then there is no ingenuity in it, except to reduce the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this method.
And it is, that you scrape the bone with iron, or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is scraped, then take an instrument according to this method, let it be a metal extremity, triangular: and its wood be a pyramid extended up to the extremity, as you see.
Then put that on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and stretch out your hand from the eye a little. Then turn your hand with the instrument, until it penetrates the bone, and the weaker one feels it: by which he finds the wind to exit from it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Then restore the place with medicines that dry and constrict, as I have taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place becomes hardened: then the matter that was flowing to the outside, returns to the nose: it is therefore lighter on the weaker one.
On the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter 20.
When the Eye protrudes with its entire mass, and no harm occurs to the sight, nor diminution: then it is necessary that you release the weaker one: then you bleed it: afterwards you put cups on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then put on the Eye an ointment that is made from acacia, and aloe, and olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the Eye above with a plaster with many powders.
And the method of bandages is: that you take soft cloths, many, and double every cloth from them over four; make many of them; then put them one on top of another, a second according to the quantity which the Eye needs; then tie over them with a hard cloth tightly bound.
And repeat the applications with suction without scarification.
Then remove the bandages and loosen the eye by day and by night.
If however the ligament is again softened, then it is necessary that it be tied much; then remove the ligament after a day and a night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye has already returned to its own tunics, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, and bandages, and binding, and applications, until it is healed.
On the incision of the conjunctiva.
Chapter 21.
When there occurs a disruption in the conjunctiva, then it protrudes and proceeds out of the eyelids like a grain of the conjunctiva, and the form of the man is disfigured because of that, and one must make an incision on it: then the operation on it is, according to what I relate; and it is, that you insert the needle at the root of the conjunctiva from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle where there is a double thread on the lacrimal side, and make it penetrate; and let the first needle remain according to its own disposition.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the conjunctiva around the needle; and tie it very tightly.
Then pull out the needle, and put over the eye a soft wool on the white of the eyeball; and leave it there until the thread falls away with the conjunctiva.
Then treat the eye with that which comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And when a suture is tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it is perforated with a subtle spatula, which penetrates to the depth of the eye, and the white humidity flows out, and the eye descends, and immediately enters: then it is tightened, until it is healed.
On the treatment of Cataracts.
Chapter 22.
This disease which is called Alcomnati, is not but a cloudiness, which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is, that you make the weak person sit on a prepared seat, or a directed one.
Then take the head of it with your hands on both sides, and move it, until you see the cloudiness penetrate to the lower parts of the eyes. Then it is fixed, and does not descend, and the light is freed: and the weak person sees things as he used to see: and if it does not descend below, know that it is water.
If however the descent of the cloudiness is not prepared with what we have said, then make the weak person sit between your hands. Then take a subtle spatula, and probe with it above the corneal membrane slightly at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the corona, until the cloudiness comes out.
When it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
On the treatment of the water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have already spoken of the type of water in division, and what is suitable for perforating, as the discourse declares and explains, then take that same thing according to health.
Then you should make the weak person sit between your hands in a square shape, opposite the light, near the torch: and bind his healthy eye, and pull it strongly: then with your right hand touch the eyelid of his eye, if the eye that has water is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take with your right hand the cotton, if the eye is the left: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the tip of the cotton near the crown through the thickness of the radius in the same white part of the eye visible with a lesser tear duct: then push the cotton with force: and you rotate it with your hand, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the cotton has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the length of the journey of the cotton to the depth be the length of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and this is the crown of the eye. For you will see it in the same pupil of the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then put the cotton to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards one after another.
If then the water descends at once, then the weak person sees that thing above which he opens his vision at once, and the cotton in his eye.
Then rest a little. If then the water descends, then put it down secondly, except that the cotton goes out.
When it is thus fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the cotton with ease: and you rotate your hand little by little.
Then dissolve in the water something from clear rock salt: and wash the eye with it. Then put outside the eye the whole poultice, or a cloth soaked in rose oil, and the whites of the eye: and tie it with that to the healthy eye.
We, however, and those who are of our time, put on top a layer of beaten cumin with the whites of the eye.
What if the magda does not penetrate to the entrance of the eye on account of its hardness, because it is from humans, whose eye is very hard: then you should take a spatula, which is called Alberid, this being its form.
Make a perforation with it therefore to the conjunctiva itself as much as possible, and do not put your hand in the opening: and it is not but that you make a way to the magda place subtly.
Then insert the magda, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your operation, prepare for the weak person a dormitory decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house: and prohibit him from all movements, and from coughing: and give him food that soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark house, and try his vision: and make him see things. Then put back the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary to do that at the time of curing, or immediately after the incision: but rather, it is necessary to avoid that: because water ascends quickly through vehement sight.
If however a hot apostema occurs: then it is necessary to release the eye before the seventh: and treat it with that which calms the apostema, until it is calmed: then free its vision, and put on its face an oral remedy which regulates its vision for some days, it being in that dark house.
Then he should go out from the house gradually and return to his own home.
And know that a disciple is not excused from witnessing several times on the operation for then he will be confident over the procedure.
And now indeed there has come to us one of them who are from Alayrach, who said that a perforated magdan was made in Alayrach through which water is sucked.
For I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of types from a magda, so that one who is ignorant of them will stand upon them.
Let them be made from their own copper: and let their extremities be with the same subtlety triangular, and the extremities pointed.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes there are born in the nose diverse fleshes added to it, among which there is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet: and from it is flesh cancerous, stone-like, of dark color: and from it is what is flesh soft, not of dark color.
In the one therefore which is from these fleshes soft, not deceptive, nor cancerous, you should make the weak one sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and thrust a hook into those fleshes: then draw them to the outside, and cut what you have grasped of them with a subtle sharp spatula on one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
What if something remains of it whose removal is not possible: then scrape that with one of the subtle instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. If however the blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs: then apply to it what is necessary: or it is one of the malignant abscesses, then cauterize him quickly, until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, should be thrown into the nose after the incision. If however the nose opens, and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then know that he is already healthy.
If however the moisture does not penetrate according to what is necessary, then know that there is rotten flesh inside it in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then therefore it is necessary that you insert a thread of linen which has some thickness, and knots on it many knots: and put between each knot the quantity of a finger, or less: and let the weak one push, so that he puts one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with what is possible for him after he made something similar to adzer: and pull his smelling thing, until it reaches up to the caassum, and comes out above his throat.
And many times indeed do boys similar to this in schools of Atto: and it is an easy thing for one who wants to.
Then put the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out above the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then as a servant cut the flesh with the sawing of the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already caught with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and put into the nose after wiping away the blood a linen cloth which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then put at the end of the thing a tin pipe into the nose, for some days, until it is healed.
If it needs a cure which will dry it out, use that.
For making a head purging, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or of brass: which should be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you want to make a closed pipe, like a reed.
And the shell-like instrument for making a head purging, should be full, and uncovered: which should have a handle at its end, with which you hold it, when you heat oil in it, or whatever you want from juices, and flowing things.
Chapter 25.
A polyp often grows at the end of the nose and increases and grows with time until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that, it is necessary to remove it at the beginning of its appearance and eradicate all of it. Then put on the place a cautery or a medicine that burns which stands in place of it.
If the cutting of it is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, of little feeling: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous aposteme.
For I have often seen that he who cut a bad aposteme, and it happened from it a great evil to the man who had it.
And if the aposteme is of a soft touch, not of a dark color, and you see that cutting is possible in all of it, then evacuate the weak part, and cut it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the place with what dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
We know that when there is a solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, the operation is not very reliable, except in certain men.
It is necessary therefore when anything of that sort happens to someone, that you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then bring the lips of the wound together with a suture, afterwards treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the separation are already separated, and each part has become healthy, then it is necessary that you should scratch out every part from its own skin until it becomes bloody. Then bring the two lips together with a suture, and tie them, and powder over it the blood of dragon, and ground frankincense: and put over the powders a piece of Diafiac plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it tied for two days, or three. Then untie it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads come apart by themselves. Then treat it with an ointment, until it heals.
The method of the suture is that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have described in the suture of the belly: or bring together the separation with a thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of knots of the lips.
Many men have lumps occurring inside their lips, hard excrescences like grains of herb, and some of them are small and others large.
Therefore it is necessary that you should turn back the lip, and find the lump, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it on every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with a powdered zeolite, until blood is stanched.
Then let it be wrapped with vinegar, and the place where there is a styptic be treated with it, until the wounds heal.
Chapter 28.
On the cutting of the flesh born above the gum.
Frequently there grows above the gum a flesh that the ancients call bullock or alveus, but which we name "addiat".
It is necessary therefore that you suspend it with a hook, or that you hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then put on the place zeeg powder crushed, or other powders styptic and astringent.
For if that flesh returns after the treatment, for it often returns, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter 29.
On the scraping of teeth with iron.
Sometimes there collect on the surfaces of teeth from the inside and outside part, and between the gums rough, foul, and blackened cortices, and sometimes they become stained, and yellow, and green, until corruption comes from that to the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
It is necessary therefore that you make the weak person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, in which there appear to you rough cortices, and things similar to sand: until nothing of them remains, and do likewise with the blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and other things until they are clean.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping over them on one day, and on a second, and on a third, until at last there comes a stop to it in that which you use.
And you should know that the tooth requires razors of diverse forms and many figures, according to which your operation is prepared: namely, that the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of razors should be prepared by you, which you will use.
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of toothache.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth for its pain with all ingenuity, and delay in extracting it. For it is not that the vicinity of the tooth supplies it when it is extracted: since it is a subtle substance: until when there is no excuse for extracting it, then it is necessary when the weak one is forced to extract it, so that it is strengthened and certified by you, the aching tooth.
For often the pain deceives the weak person, and he thinks it is in a sound tooth and extracts it: then the pain does not go away, until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen that from the operation of bloodletting practitioners.
When therefore the aching tooth is certified by you itself, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides: then move it with your fingers or with subtle forceps at first little by little, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in it, with good positioning, and hold the weak head between your knees: which you have already held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth according to straightness, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under the tooth from all sides with ease, and constrain its movement, as you did at first.
If the dense is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with a cloth and tie it with a thin thread of a radius, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction on it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act carefully with scarification around the gum on all sides abundantly.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and it returns over the weakness from that evil which is greater than its pain at first.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness over the eradication of it, beyond what we have administered.
For they often cause great weaknesses to come upon men, which are difficult to break, and all the roots of it remain, or some of them: or so that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have often testified to that.
Then let it be glued after the eradication of it with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
What if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it often happens) then take something of gum and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the gum does not adhere to you.
The shape of the thin forceps which move the dense at first, should be of long extremities, short handles, thick, so that they do not double when pressed with them on a tooth: they should be as you see of thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not release themselves, nor double: of short extremities, and let them be made from iron or wrought and properly made iron, with straight extremities: and in their extremities let there be teeth, some of which enter into others, and squeeze with a decent constriction, strongly.
And when the extremities are formed in the shape of a parrot's beak, which is called ciconia, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
On the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jaw bones.
When something remains after the eradication of a tooth that breaks, then it is necessary to put a cloth soaked in butter on the spot for one or two days, until the area softens: then insert into it forceps or tongs: whose extremities are similar to a parrot's beak which is called ciconia. This form indeed is of the forceps.
Let their extremities be made like file or like toothpicks.
If then it does not come out for you with these forceps, then it is necessary that you dissecet above the root and remove all the flesh, and insert the instrument which is called a small lever, whose shape is this.
Let its extremity be short, somewhat thick: and let it not be fragile, so that it does not break.
If then the root comes out through that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to use with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first one has a triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of the triangular extremity is slender.
And with other instruments and iron tools which preceded in the rasping of teeth.
And also use with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this shape, triangular twisted extremity, in which there is a certain thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be fragile.
And know that the instruments of teeth are many: and similarly the rest of the instruments are not accounted for, and the skilled artisan, subtle in his art, finds by himself the instrument, according to which he signifies to himself the operation and the difficulties.
Since there are pains for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, because of the diversity of their species.
If the bone is broken from the jaw or from other bones of the mouth, or becomes putrid: then probe over it in its place with that which suits it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a gest, whose form is this.
Let there be in it something of thickness little by little, so that it may be tightened, or the bone may be held with it, and it does not flee and is steady, until the bone goes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
If however there is rottenness in the bone, then scrape the bone itself from its rottenness, and cure it, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
On the sawing of prominent teeth above the others.
When teeth do not protrude according to their natural course, a foul shape is made by it: and especially when this happens in women, and girls, namely those with venereal ailments.
It is necessary therefore that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and does not have a place, or it is possible for its sawing, nor its smoothing: then extract it: and if it is connected to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from Indian iron, of sharp extremity, strongly.
And let your cutting be that which you cut with it for many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another of the teeth is not disturbed.
If however it is prominent and exposed for its smoothing: then smooth it with a file made from Indian iron, whose form is this.
Let it be entirely made from Indian iron, and its handle smaller by a few points, strongly: like a file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is smoothed little by little over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls: then clean it, and finally scrape it with certain scrapers.
What if it is dense, from which a part will be broken and it injures the tongue: then it is necessary to smooth it also, until the roughness of that breaking is removed, and it is made even, and flattened, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter 33.
Of the gold wire filling of teeth.
When the front teeth undergo a concussion and commotion from a blow or a fall: and the weak one cannot chew on what he eats so that it does not fall: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment was not effective: then the idea for them is to be fastened with a gold wire: because silver turns green and putrefies after days: but gold never changes.
And let the thread be moderate in fineness and thickness according to the quantity that it can hold between the teeth.
And the form of the filling is that you take the thread and insert double its length between two healthy teeth: then weave with both ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether it is one or several, until you come with the web to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving until the part from which you began: and pull your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved forward: and let your constriction by which you tighten the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not protrude.
Then cut the overflowing ends of the thread with scissors: and gather both, and shape them with forceps: and hide them between a healthy tooth and a moved tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue: then let it be thus tightened while it remains.
If however it becomes loose or is cut, tighten them with another thread, and it will stand with that for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the filling of two healthy teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And when it happens that one or two teeth fall out after the removal of each and they are retained in their places as we have described, and remain; and this is not done except by a skilled and experienced artisan.
And when something is fitted from the bones of the jaw and made into the shape of a tooth, and put in the place where the tooth was lost and tied as we have said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes the ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already consolidated.
And the operation on it is to open the mouth of the weak one on your lap, and elevate their tongue: then cut that nervous ligament across its width, until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
What if there is something hard and knotted in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then put a hook into it, and cut across its width, until you cut the ligament and the knot is loosened.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you cut the artery there, and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then let the area of the incision be colluted with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then put under the tongue a plaster of flax, which the weak one should hold all night, so that it does not consolidate again.
If however there occurs a flow of blood, then put on the place powdered gum. If the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a lenitive cauter, which is suitable for it: then treat it with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
