Then cut the ends of the needles, so that they do not injure the weak place at its sleep: and put pillows made of cloth on every side, which hold the ends of the needles: and leave them, until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this kind of suture with needles is more suitable for small wounds. For one or two needles, or around them, are sufficient for the suture.
The second common suture is to gather with the suture four ends: two ends of the skin, and two ends of the thread in place of one, with a needle in which there is an equal thread in fineness and thickness.
Then when you penetrate with the needle these four ends, bring the needle back from the side where you began, so that the tied thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that it is a suture, like the suture of cloth that clothes are stitched with, and put between every suture the thickness of a smaller finger.
Men know this suture.
And with this suture I myself sewed up a wound that had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a major disruption of the wound the size of one palm, and about two palms had come out of his intestines from the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the belly.
I put back that which after it had stood, his intestine outside of the wound for 24 hours.
The wound was then consolidated in the space that it had been, for about 15 days: and I treated it, and it healed: and he lived after that for many years, living out his natural term.
And the doctors judged that it would not heal.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with an ointment. For I was in a place where no ointment was to be found, nor any of the medicines. I put a old cloth over the wound, twice a day, and I persevered with washing it with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two proper types of suture according to the account of the discourse.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture, and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When the needle thus penetrates the skin, and into the flesh equally along the length and width of the whole body: leave the thread end on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer in the other flesh on the opposite side.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this same side of the flesh from the outer part to the inner: and leave the thread end which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other side from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration that you make it penetrate in the thread end of the flesh, which is on its side, until it penetrates into the whole.
Then seize also from this same side and its extremity, which is the spleen on the other side, and extract the needle from the skin that it has penetrated.
Then reduce it to that skin, and its extremity of the spleen, which is on the other side, with this extremity of the spleen: and extract it from the skin which is on its side, and make one suture, and another: and make that suture after the suture, until you have sewn all of it according to that example.
And the sum of this suture, is that you should sew like those who sew pelles: so that you have one suture on one side of one extremity of the skin: and that you should pass it to the other side, until you complete.
The second type of its own suture, which Galenus called.
This speech of his has been related, that they sew it according to a likeness, which some physicians do, so that they bring every part to its neighboring part fitting by nature: and apply the extremity of the spleen to its other extremity, and the extremity of the spleen to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I relate to you.
It is necessary that you thrust the needle into the extremity of the spleen nearest to you from the outer part, and make it penetrate to the interior in that alone: and let two extremities of the spleen pass through. Then turn the needle around, and make it penetrate from the outer part to the interior in the two extremities of the spleen, namely on both sides.
Then turn both around, and also make both penetrate from the interior to the exterior, into the two other extremities of the spleen, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of sewing is better than the common and easy suture, and it is a suture whose mention came before.
This is the treatise of Galen.
And some have said. The suture of the wound is reinforced with two stitches. That is, you insert into them a single thread: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew skins equally.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the wound, then its suture is more difficult than that of the other places of the wound.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of the rest of the wounds.
That is, because when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and sew it, and adapt it: then put on top an incarnative powder.
That if the air has already altered the wound: then put on top of it certain ointments, which are present to you, until they produce pus: and fall apart the threads, and consolidate the flesh, and form a scar. Then treat it as is the treatment of the other wounds, until it heals.
If however the medicines are not present to you, then put on top of it immediately, from which it begins to emit pus, an old dressing: and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.
For you do not apply other medications in many treatments, when the wounds are simple.
If however you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary to immerse a woolen thread tempered with warm oil, or in rose oil: and put it around the places that are between the groin, and under the armpits.
That if it feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in its intestines (for many times this happens) then clysterize it with a styptic wine, black, warm: especially if the putrefaction has already penetrated to the intestine, and an ulcer has been made which penetrates to its concavity.
And you should know that what is from the thick intestine is of easier treatment and what is from the thin is of more difficult treatment.
The intestine which is called the Jejunum does not receive treatment from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, that it is from many veins, and their greatness, and because of the thinness of its body, and because of the closeness of its nature to the nerves.
If what proceeds from the wound is feces, and you catch it fresh: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
If indeed it has passed beyond the space, and already it has become green or black: then it is necessary that you constrict it with a thread above the place which has become discolored from it, so that there does not occur a flow of blood. For in the intestine there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and put two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it: so that the pulling of it may be alleviated by you, and the extraction of it in case of intestine, and the emission of pus of the wound.
The treatment of a wound which occurs in the Intestine.
When there occurs a disruption in the Intestine and it is small: then it is possible that it be restored in some men: because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a thrust of a lance, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound became chronic, and a fistula was formed, and there came out from it a discharge and gas.
I applied therefore the treatment, until I was not confident in its healing: nor did I cease to treat it subtly, until it was healed and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the weak, that which would happen to the weak would befall a disease in its stomach: and there did not befall it from that a befalling disease. And it remained in its better dispositions, eating and drinking, and blowing, and it entered a bath, and was exercised in its diets.
And now certain experimenters have said, that when a wound happens in the intestine and is small: then it should be tied according to this method. And it is, that you take the poultices of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and put one poultice among those that have an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When it has seized upon them, and closed its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and is not loosened.
Then put another poultice near the first, and do not cease to do that with a poultice after a poultice according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it itself, and its own wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine, until the intestine is healed and cured, and no harm befalls the weak.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied with a subtle thread: which is taken from the intestine of an animal, and attached to it after it has been thrust into a needle.
And it is, that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and much flesh comes out. Then tie at its end a subtle flaxen thread wound around. Then thrust that thread into the needle, and sew up the intestine with it. Then reduce it itself to a push.
And this method of sewing with poultices and intestine is not but according to the way of trust and hope.
One knows a wound that is great and large, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines: then there is no ingenuity except subtlety, and there is not its healing at all.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and fistulas.
Know that every wound or abscess when it becomes old and chronic, and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and always discharges pus, and does not close: then it is universally called a fistula in whichever member it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula is indeed a hard, white, calloused and blocked nodulation, with which there is no pain, to which is similar the hollow of a bird's feather: and because of that some people call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is in certain hours wet, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is discharged at certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and is little: and it is thick, and it is subtle.
And I have already stated in the division, then when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor is born in it flesh, is not that except on account of one of nine causes.
One of which is, or on account of the smallness of blood in the body: or on account of its malice: or because between it and above its lips there is hard flesh, preventing the birth of flesh: or because it is much filth and foulness: or because the ulcer in itself is rotten, and the matter which is applied to it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is inconvenient for its cure: or because corruption falling in the region is a type of pestilence, or by property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which difficult digestion of fevers occurs, and delayed healing of abscesses by nature: or because in it there is one mouth, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division; and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For some of them are Fistulas that pass to the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the diaphragm, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the composite joints: such as the hand, and foot: and some of them are which approach nearness of the principal member, and similar things.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is in this manner, is from diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive cure, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
What if it is from the Fistulas any of whose depth does not penetrate to these places which I said: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and you use to penetrate to the knowledge of it, then take a probe made of brass, or iron, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and inquire into it with it.
If however in the Fistula there is crookedness, then investigate it with a probe of refined lead. For lead, with gentleness of its body, enters and bends around the crookedness.
What if the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to investigate about it with a probe. Therefore stop up one of its openings from its own openings.
For the moisture that you stop it with, goes towards the other openings, and flows out from them.
Then carefully and precisely inquire according to which method it is possible for you: to know what that is a hole, or a nerve, and is the long or short concavity of the Fistula: or is one Fistula with many openings, and stand on all that, with the greatest strength of yours: with your questioning, with which you question the weak, and the discovery of pain upon compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and causes occurring in the examination.
When you stand upright over all those stations of urine, then proceed to cure according to confidence: and it is, as you consider then, if there is a fistula appearing nearby or in a healthy place far from the joint, or from a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places that I told you: then cut the fistula according to what was previously mentioned in my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupted flesh, which grows in its opening from flesh, or thelul, and similar things: and cure it, until it is healed.
If the fistula is of deep and distant depth, and is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it as deeply as is possible for you. Then cleanse it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply unbroken bandages in sharp medicines: and insert them up to the concavity of the fistula which you grasped with the iron: do that repeatedly, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remained in the concavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If it does not heal by means of that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
When the cause of the fistula is an opening, and it is confirmed by you: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I have narrated to you: if nothing prohibits you from the vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
When the opening is detected by you, and there is in it something of corruption, and blackness: then scrape it, until all its corruption is removed. Then cure it with what makes flesh grow on it, until it is healed.
What if it does not heal, and discharges pus as it was doing: then know that you did not attain the complete eradication of the corruption.
Cut down on that second, and make a careful incision of it, and cleansing of the same with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If then it is healed, it is good: and if not, then you should know that the corruption is in the depth of the body, which you have not reached. There is therefore no ingenuity for you in it, except to entrust it to nature.
What is corrupted of the small mouth, and it is possible for you, draw it out with subtle forceps that convene to it.
If however there are many bones, then make a careful pulling of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If indeed something from the humidity makes an obstacle for you, and there is no fear of incision or error: then cut those humidities and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If indeed you are not prepared to remove the bones from the hour of your treatment, then apply on the wound what will putrefy it: and leave it, until it putrefies what is around those bones of flesh and humidity.
And guard the wound so that it does not consolidate. And let the section which you cut in your operation be bound with a bandage. Below put on it, if you wish, cotton submerged in sulphur beaten with oil: or cotton submerged in Egyptian ointment, or some of the green ointments.
For when those humidities putrefy, and the bones are separated from them, the removal of them will be facilitated, and their attraction.
If however there is one large bone, such as the bone of the thigh, or the hip bone, and similar to these, and a large part of it is corrupted: then scrape it with a final scraping, until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If the bone which is in it has become corrupted, and is a large part, and the corruption has already affected the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it entirely up to the place where the corruption penetrates: then treat it, until it consolidates.
And I will tell you a story about a man who had an injury on his thigh: so that you may take it as an example and aid to your operation.
There was a man of youthful age, about thirty years old, to whom pain occurred in his thigh, from a cause which moved upon it within the body, that is, from the interior, until the materials reached the thigh and it became an aposteme, a large aposteme: and there was not an external cause for him.
The time passed with it until the error of the doctors, until the aposteme opened, and many materials flowed out with it. And the treatment of it was put off, until the thigh became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus, and the bodily fluids.
A multitude of doctors treated him for about two years, and there was no one skilled in the art among them, until I was called, and I saw his thigh, and the materials which flowed out from those openings with a great flow: also the whole body of the man had already wasted away, and its color was citrine.
I then inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached to the bone.
Then I thoroughly examined all the openings: and I found that they connected to a sinus coming from all parts of the thigh.
I cut around one of those openings, until I exposed a piece of bone: and I found it corrupted, which had already been gnawed away, and was blackened, and putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I will scrape off what has been detected by me and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I scraped off what was not in the bone except that corruption which I cut away and scraped. And I will now eradicate it.
Then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it is not consolidated.
Then I felt and probed on the bone a second time above the first detection, and I found continuous corruption with the bone, and I scraped off what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
I probed then on it again, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut out about one palm from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed: and this iteration in the operation of it, and its section, would not have been necessary, except because of the disposition of debility and infirmity, and the smallness of its tolerance, and my fear concerning it of death: for it happened to him in every hour from an excess of evacuating bad syncopy.
He was healed therefore by a complete healing: and hard flesh was born in the place of the bone, and good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he changed in his dispositions: and walking did not cause him any harm at all.
If the bone is prominent in the place of the body and already broken, then it is necessary to scrape it, according to this method: and it is that you take a ligature, and tie it on the extremity of the prominent bone: and tell someone to pull it upwards: and put another ligature made from coarse wool over the first ligature. Then tie it above the flesh that is under the bone and stretch out the extremity so that you pull the flesh to the lower parts and you cut the flesh above the place you want to saw, so that the saw does not wound the flesh.
Put a stick or a board under the lower bone decently: because when you do that, it is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be above the corrupted place for a little while, so that the corruption is not in the concavity of the bone, and does not appear in its appearance to the sense, and compels to saw itself instead of another.
Which if the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, namely the continuous part of it with a part: and the corruption is in the middle of it or in a part of it: then cut the flesh from all parts entirely. Then put a stick below.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues from the other part: and let the sawing be according to the length from the corruption for a little while, according to what we said.
Which if the corruption is in the joint, cut out the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
If indeed the corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no ingenuity in it except for scraping.
Which if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or in the sole of the foot: then the matter in it is very difficult. It is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever ingenuity is straightened for you: when the vein or nerve does not make a contradiction for you.
And know that the incisions and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the situation of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and subtlety, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Wherefore it is necessary that you prepare every kind of instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations signify to you above the species of an instrument which you need when there is lengthy study with you. And the knowledge of the modes of this art is a testimony of the modes of diseases: then indeed you will find by yourself that which suits it from instruments for every weakness.
And I will establish for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will pose as examples on which you will stumble, and a test by which you will experience regarding others.
The shape of another saw.
The shape of a razor for joints. Let the head of this razor be according to the shape of the head of a star-shaped key: and its points be according to the shape of the points of a lancet.
And it does not fit, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they are corroded, or the mouth is wide and large.
The shape of a larger razor.
The shape of a broad razor.
The shape of a razor in which there is a cavity.
The shape of another subtle razor.
The shape of a razor with a curved end.
The shape of a small razor, of subtle handling.
This is similar to a file, and the extremities are like a saw: it is shown in many places from the rasping of bones.
The form of a rasp that suits the rasping of that which is perforated by bones.
Its extremities are triangular, and it is sharp at the extremities.
It is made from Indian steel. And it is similarly necessary that all the things we have spoken of be made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
Of the cutting of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
The extremities rot, either from an external cause or an internal one.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medical cures, and the cure is not effective: and you see the corruption falling upon a member, which nothing has struck: then it is necessary that you cut into that member as far as the corruption has reached, so that the weak man may be freed from death, or from an evil which is a greater defect of the member.
And the sign to which this happens is that the member becomes blackened, until it rots: as if fire has consumed it, or it has rotten after the blackening: until the rottenness is cut into, as far as that which follows the member, and creeps into the whole body.
It is suitable therefore with the cutting of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain poisonous things: such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and weasel, and similar things.
If therefore the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then cut into the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, so that it may not proceed as far as the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it out from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it invades into it, then cut the arm at the elbow at its joint.
If on the other hand corruption passes through and you see it around the elbow then there is death of the weak limb. And to administer beyond that from the cure, is a quantity of virtues.
And do similarly for the foot, when corruption invades into the toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold in the body of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
What if corruption has already gone beyond the knee, then there is no ingenuity in it, except to let go and abandon the weak one to death.
And the method of cutting a limb or sawing it is to tie a ligature below where you want to cut, and tie another ligature above the place. And let one assistant stretch one ligature downwards to the lower parts and let another assistant stretch the upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures with a broad spatula until the whole flesh is laid bare. Then let it be cut or sawed.
And it is fitting that you put a linen cloth on all parts, and the saw does not touch the healthy place: and an injury added to the weak one, and a hot abscess.
If however there is an issue of a flow of blood internally while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put on it certain powders that stop the blood. Then return to your medication until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a bandage suitable for it, and take care of it, until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man with his foot, the same thing that I am telling you.
What is it, that a blackness occurs on its foot with a burning which is similar to the burning of fire and that corruption first occurs on its toe until it affects the whole foot.
It put out a man from itself, after it had seen the corruption befalling the limb with its swelling, which it found from the pain and burning of it, and it befalls at the joint and it was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, that same corruption occurred to him on the index finger of his hand.
He came to me and I attempted to cure that excess with what I applied on the hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body, and the excess did not push back, and it began to enter into another finger until the corruption began in the hand.
He called for me to cut his hand and I was unwilling to do it, hoping for a pushing back of that excess from me, and I also feared death for him in the cutting of his hand. For the man's strength was on the case.
After he had given up hope in me, he returned to his land, and he came back to me because of that, which he had done hastily, and he cut his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I did not add this account except as an aid for that which falls from the type of this disease and as a signification which is signified and according to which it is acted.
On the treatment of abscesses, and the quality of cleansing with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends until it makes a collection of pus: then it breaks or is perforated, and what is in it of pus comes out, and the empty place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it like cloth that has already been worn thin: and it does not reach to the end in its corruption, nor does it press on the bone, nor on the nerve, nor on the ligament: it deserves to be called an abscess: and it is not called a Fistula: nevertheless if its space extends until the corruption presses on any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or a false passage.
And the treatment of it is that you cut the entire wound as it was previously turned out, and especially when it has been subtly divided and made like a cloth, and you are confident because it will not adhere to the place due to its own corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and it does not follow from it the last corruption of the union, and if there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster, and it is so that you should consider regarding the sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it is of foul odor, then clysterize it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ginger, taking equal parts, mixed together in a vase, and decocted over fire, until the medicine turns red, and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need, and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the sinus with it, and constrict its opening, and put the medicine into it for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract it with expression: do this for as many days as until the sinus is cleaned and the foul odor is removed.
And when you clysterize with water of ash, when this medicine is not present to you, and it is that you take the ash of grapevines or the ash of holm oak trees, and cast water upon it. Then mix it and clysterize with it, until you are confident that the sinus has already been washed out.
What if the weak one is not able to tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor water of ash, then clysterize it with water and honey, in which something has already been mixed from crushed ginger. Or clysterize it with honey and wine mixed together, since it is a property of honey that it cleanses and washes, and the wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of dryness and astringency in the wine.
When you do that several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has already been removed: then put a suppository on it along with what makes flesh grow in it.
And it is that you take from the plaster dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil and styptic wine, or put a suppository on it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
What if the orifice of the sinus is constricted, in which the cannula of the clyster does not fit: then widen it with iron for a little while: or put a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment or in royal ointment on it, until it is widened.
And similarly if the orifice is also too wide, then bring its lips together with a suture: and reduce from it the quantity in which the clyster can enter without obstruction.
And similarly if the orifice from which pus runs is elevated upwards: then make an incision in the lower place that is in it, so that the pus may run out downwards: for when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated in it.
What if it is not possible for you to make an incision in the sinus around its lower part according to what you want: then try to prepare a membrane with a preparation so that pus may run out of it with ease according to what you have prepared. Try that nothing of the pus remains in it at all: and take consolidating medicines which are put on the wound, suitable for what you want. And spread them on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it makes contact with the whole sinus. Then cut with forceps directly at the orifice of the sinus a hole, slightly larger than the orifice of the sinus. Then spread from mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole: and put it over the orifice, positioned lengthwise away from the orifice of the sinus, so that the exit of pus from it is eased: and do not remove the large cloth, except after a few days, once.
That one, however, which is above the orifice of the sinus, is that which you remove at every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus may exit: and so that you know, whether that which flows out from it is a lot, or a little: or digested, or undigested.
And with this again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether the master of it feels pain in it, or there is not pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or there is not an abscess in it.
When therefore your medication is thus extended, and you see the material which exits above the orifice of the sinus, then know that the sinus already adheres, or approaches adherence: then therefore loosen it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you loosen the wound, then change the dressing to another, and renew the ointment.
If however there exits from the sinus after a long space subtle pus, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, since there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act to the contrary of that.
If however there exits from the sinus after many days undigested pus, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adherence of the sinus is delayed, and its condition is prolonged: then add to the desiccation of medicines, true ones which doctors make, so that they are medicines in their own humid substance, and in their own dry virtue: such as the ointment dyafinicon, when there is added to it an excess of addition from zegi.
And of the greatest last thing by which it is cured, is that you take the ointment dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil, and apply upon it old equal wine in its own substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now perform a similar action to this, when you do not have the ointment of dyafinicon present, melt the honey, when you have decocted it, until it becomes thick and anoint with it. Take myrrh and aloe and benzoin: grind all together, and pulverize it over the honey while it is over the fire. Then smear over it a cloth, and bind it over the wound: and anoint it with honey which you have decocted, until it has thickened: and pulverize the spices over it, and put over it a cloth, and bind them.
And when the treatment is administered there is aristolochia longa, and roots of celestial lily, and it is lily: and flour of the herb, and centaury. Use them simple, or mixed, grind them, and sieve them: and pulverize over the honey which I have described, and use it.
If the matter of the wound should be prolonged, and it is not healed by this treatment: then know that there is a corruption in its depth: or now it makes an impression on the bones, or on the remaining hard members, or on the ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of Fistulas, according to what has been previously stated.
Of the treatment of a panaritium and a worn away nail, and an incised toe, and a cut.
A panaritium is an abscess that grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it grows on the other toes.
When it is prolonged and the treatment of it is neglected, and it becomes an abscess with a hot abscess: or it becomes rotten, and it emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it entirely rots it, and perhaps the corruption reaches to the bone, until it has a foul smell: and the extremity of the toe becomes large, and its color becomes dark.
When you raise your treatment with what we said in the division, and your treatment does not work: then it is necessary to cut with iron all the excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cauterization is a strong remedy.
If indeed the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the additions of empty nail now press on the flesh inside, and begin to pierce it, and wound it: then it is necessary that you put a fine pumice under the angle of the nail which pierces the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut that flesh with ease, and put on top of what remains of the flesh from medicinally caustic and corrosive remedies, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If however the corruption has already made an impression on the bone, then it is necessary that you cut out that bone, and extract it: for it will not be healed as long as the corrupted bone remains in it.
What if you see corrosion and corruption entering into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to which a previous mention has been made. Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however a bruise of the nail or a blow occurs, and there is severe pain in it, then it is necessary to perform bloodletting on the weak part first. Then cut the nail with a sharp razor from top to bottom across.
And beware, lest the cut penetrate to the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause through that a severe pain on the weak part, and it will be a cause for the growth of extra flesh in the location.
Then treat the place with what soothes pains.
The finger which is added to the birth in some hands of men is perhaps entirely fleshy and perhaps there are bones in some of them and perhaps there is a nail in it and some of them are born at the root of the joint or they are born in some nodes of the finger. That one which is born at some nodes of the finger does not move and that one which is born at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
The cutting of that one which of them is fleshy is easy. That is, you cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
The treatment of that one whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore abstain from cutting it.
In that one which is born on the finger at the last node, then it is necessary to cut the flesh first with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that purpose. Then treat it until it is healed.
The joining together which occurs to the fingers, often happens either from the birth of the man, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from the burning of fire, and similar things.
It is necessary therefore to cut away that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then put between them lint or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them, or put between them a subtle sheet of lead, until the place is consolidated according to what is necessary.
And similarly if there occurs a joining to some fingers with the palm, then cut away that joining according to what prepares and suits the shape of the member.
On the Incision of a Varicose Vein.
There are varicose veins, which are enlarged, and full of melancholic superfluities, which occur in many parts of the body: and they most often happen in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers and laborers.
It is necessary therefore to administer a strong purging of the body from black bile by repeated strong purging.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient in that vein which is called basilica.
The treatment of them with an iron instrument is according to two methods. One of them is, that they should be cut and black blood extracted. And the other method is, that the vein should be lifted up, and entirely extracted.
The cutting of it is according to this method. First immerse the leg in warm water abundantly, until the gross and foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the weak leg from the upper part of its thigh to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then lift, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above it is necessary that you should perform bloodletting on the weak one first. Then cut the vein to the lower part until a quantity of blood comes out which you see is sufficient and which the strength of the weak one can tolerate.
Afterwards bind it, and instruct him to abstain from foods which generate black bile, and also evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and that harms the weak one.
The lifting of it is according to this method. Scrape the weak leg, if there are many hairs on it. Then insert it into a bath, or soak it in its sheath with hot water until it becomes red and the veins become swollen, or let it be exercised with strong exercise if a bath is not available to it until the limb becomes warm.
Then cut the skin in the direction of the vein's section along its length, or at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and clear the vein from every part until it becomes apparent to sensation, and at its appearance, that which you see is a decline from redness to blackness. When it emerges from the skin, you see it as a white thread, as if it were a tendon.
Then insert the needle under it, until when it is raised and comes out of the skin: suspend it with a blunt hook, gently.
Then cut another section near that section, three fingers apart. Then clear the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it becomes apparent. Then raise it with the needles as you have done, and suspend it with another hook, as you have done at first.
Then cut another section, or several sections, if it is necessary for that purpose. Then raise and cut it at the last section near the heel.
Then pull it out to the section which is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the section which is higher, or higher than all sections, until when the whole comes out: then cut it.
If it does not obey you to be pulled out and raised: then insert a needle with a strong thread double, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert the needle under it: and move your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it will be difficult for you to elevate it, and from it an injury will occur to the weak part.
When you have extracted it entirely, then apply to the wound a wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and treat it until it is healed.
What if the vein is contracted, having a twisted condition with an involvement of parts, and it is not as we have said according to rectitude: then it is necessary that you find on it for all the part of its twisting, and the place of its involvement.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you elevate it entirely: and beware at your dissection above it lest you cut the vein, or wound: for it will be difficult for you to elevate, or extract it. Preserve therefore your caution.
The shape of the incision by which a vein is healed.
The shape of a blind hook.
It does not have curvature like the rest of the hooks, nor is it of sharp extremity, so that it does not wound the vein: and it is of thick flexibility, gentle: for if it were subtle, it would cut the vein with its subtlety: indeed let it be superfluous grossness, as we have said.
On the extraction of the saphena vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Alizegi, and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, in which no herb or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as occur in bodies of serpents and worms, ascarids, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the inception of this vein is a severe inflammation which occurs in the thigh. Then the place should be warmed. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that warming, as if it were a root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead, whose weight is one or two drachms, and tie it, and let the suspended lead hang from the thigh.
Whenever therefore something comes out from it to the outside, wrap that in lead, and tie it. What if it is prolonged a great deal, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root until the whole thing has come out. For if you cut it, it contracts and returns into the flesh: and causes an aposteme to form, and decay in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Wherefore it should be treated gently and extracted gradually, until it comes out entirely from its end, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein comes out in some men what is five palms long. And now it has happened to me that a man came out with it twenty palms long.
If however it is cut during the time of your treatment: then introduce the needle into its opening, and perforate it with a long needle, with a thread, until everything that is in it of material comes out: and administer putrefaction to the place with medicines for some days. Then treat it with the cure for apostemes.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle of the foot, or in the foot itself: and there are many openings to it, and a branch comes out from each opening.
Treat it itself, as we have said in the division, and with what has been mentioned beforehand.
On the excision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine disease.
This disease is called bovine disease in a certain land among us: therefore, because it often happens to cattle, and there is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it wanders throughout the whole body, going up and down: and it is shown to the sense during its movement from limb to limb, until it breaks out wherever it breaks out on the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. As worms and snakes happen, that is, intestinal worms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared on account of its wickedness. For when it wanders in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches the place: then perhaps it opens in it, and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
When therefore you wish to cure it, and extract it, then it is not done unless during its movement, and the appearance of it to the senses.
It is necessary therefore, that you tie what is above it, and what is below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it is deep in the flesh, and you do not find it: then put a cauterization with fire on the place, until it dies.
And what is feared most, is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then tie beneath it the eyebrow with a good tight bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is fitting that the weak person should use a cleansing of his body with medicines that dissolve rotten and foul humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Of the cutting upon the disease of Nakir.
This disease is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain which occurs in certain members, then it changes from member to member.
And I indeed have already seen it, according to what I relate to you. I was called to a weak woman in certain villages. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw a swelling enter with the tendon, as the worm enters, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be, so that it is like living silver, when it runs from place to place.
The pain therefore receded from that place and became fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it wandered throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the swiftness of its change from member to member. And I indeed had not seen this disease before with my eyes in this manner. However I saw many who find the pain changing from member to member, and I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is anything but because she was one of those who are from villages with a body dried out by detected veins, hence the changed condition appears to the sense.
And it is necessary that they do not appear according to this method in those who are delicate and have humid bodies of hidden veins.
When you apply the treatment and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to your eye, as we have said, then tie above it and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that constricted windiness comes out: and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with purging of the body, and with that which expels windiness, and cleanses them with foul-smelling pills, and pills of serapion, and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are differentiated according to their own kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is this: that among them are those which have large and small heads, and are concave, and obverse. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those which have barbs. And among them are those which have stars or studs.
Those however which are according to the limbs in which they fall, are according to two modes. For either the limbs are from the principal hollow members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and their likes. When therefore it falls into any of these members, and death signs appear to you, which I will tell you afterwards, then it is necessary that you should hasten the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in most cases. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, are: because there occur from that swelling of the brain, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflation, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and voice is destroyed. And a white humidity similar to poultice exits from the place of the wound. And there exits from it something similar to the washing of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from cure, and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left breast: and he feels with it as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the motion of a pulse: and black blood runs out from the wound. And it is followed by coldness of the extremities, and a cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung, is the exit of foamy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck become swollen, and the color of the weak person changes. And he exhales a great, high breath: and seeks an attraction of cold air.
For if the arrow falls into the membrane, which is in the breast: then it is near the short ribs: and there is made a great panting with vehement pain, with sighing: and all the members of the shoulders are moved.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps it will come out of the wound with some undigested food.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and is fixed in it, something of dung will come out of the wound, or a piece of the intestine, or the gut which has already been torn. There is therefore no hope in its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
What if an arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger over the weaker parts, then you should know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder, and arm, and spine of the back, and elbow, and hip, and leg, and their likes among the limbs are saved according to various dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve: and it is not a poisonous arrow.
And I relate to you what I have seen concerning the removal of these arrows, so that you may understand through it about your cure.
Which is, because to a certain man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye below his lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not take out more of it except a small end, with which it adhered to its wooden shaft: and it was a large arrow among the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there were no notches.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and this is that which has barbs. I cut on the same thing between two fingers, and now it was submerged in his throat. I was careful with it, until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man into whom it had fallen in his belly, and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed for him, and nothing of his condition had changed, I cut above the arrow, and I probed above it, and I extracted it, and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound was consolidated above it. Afterwards it was after seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound was consolidated above it, and the arrow remained inside her, and nothing of her condition was changed, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen into his face, and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, not finding much pain for him, and many things similar to these.
And I extracted an arrow from one of the king's nobles, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and it had declined to the right side a little, and the arrow was totally hidden.
I was called then to his treatment after the accident of the arrow for three days. I found the wound of the arrow constricted strongly.
I examined it with a subtle probe, and I did not feel it, and he himself found puncture and pain under his right armpit. The spear was removed, therefore that puncture was from the extremity of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the spot with a plaster in which there was an attractive and digestive virtue, trusting with me that it would bring forth the place and the sign of the arrow would appear to me so I could cut above it, and no accident happened in the place indicating that the arrow had contaminated the spot.
I treated it diligently with a plaster for many days, and no accident occurred, and the wound was consolidated meanwhile; and he remained weak from the extraction of that space for some days, until he felt the arrow on a certain day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I applied therefore to that wound a sharp medicine, corroding for many days, until it was opened: and when I probed it, I felt the extremity of the arrow subtle which adhered to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us: and he stayed with me for a space of time around four months.
After the wound had been enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and to move it, and it did not obey for exit: then I did not cease to subtly contrive, and to ingeniously work on it with modes of ingenuity, until I began on it one day with well-made forceps, according to which the shape of them will come in the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I restored it therefore, and the wound was consolidated, and the weak one was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I will tell you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you put that rule and meaning on that which I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not understood with narration: nor does the book comprehend it, and the skilled artisan compares a little with a lot: and with what is present, above that which is absent: and finds a new operation, and a new instrument in the descent of extraneous things of this art when they occur.
I say therefore, that arrows do not come out of the members in which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by traction from the place into which it entered. Or by the opposite part of another.
And that which comes out from where it entered, is an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place: and is drawn, and comes out.
For if it does not obey to exit at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its attraction is alleviated, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and does not obey you to exit, then leave it also for some days: and move it with attraction and motion every day, until it comes out.
For if it does not obey you to exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone on every side with a subtle drill, until the arrows are freed. Then draw it out, and extract it.
If the arrow has already been concealed in a location of the body, and is hidden and concealed from sensation: then investigate it with probing. If you feel it, then attract it with certain instruments that are suitable for its attraction.
If you cannot reach it, on account of the constriction of the wound, and on account of the length of the arrow in the depth: and there is not there a bone, nor a nerve, nor a vein: then probe above it, until the wound is enlarged, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
What if there are to it nerves that hold it, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side, with every device that you can, and devices, if it cannot overcome the protrusion of the flesh in the fracture of the ears, and the twisting of them, until it comes out.
And you when you administer the extraction of the arrow, in whatever place it is, then use twisting of your hand, with forceps on all parts, until you make it come out: and manipulate with ultimate subtlety, so that the arrow is not fractured, and its attraction and extraction are not made difficult for you.
What if you cannot overcome it in one hour, then leave it for some days, until those things that are around it putrefy. Then repeat it: for the tunic will be loosened, indeed if a flow of blood makes a contrary condition for you, then administer what we have said from the cure in its chapter: and keep your strength away from the incision of the vein, or nerve, or cord.
And administer the ingenuity in every way that is possible to you for the removal of the arrowhead: and let it be with facility, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have narrated to you.
And it is necessary, that you do when you pull out the arrow, that the weak part be according to the figure, according to which it was in the case of the arrow in itself. For it is a greater help. If however it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
The arrow which comes out from the opposite part of another, or already proceeds from it something to the exterior: or makes a gibbosity of the extremity of the arrow by sensation from the upper part of the adjacent skin, and you see it prominent: cut therefore over it, and let the section be according to the quantity that the forceps can grasp in it.
Then pull out the arrow: for it itself becomes easy to exit.
If indeed it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness, until the arrow presses into the bone, and enlarges itself: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for some days: then repeat it, until it comes out.
What if the wood of the arrow is in it, then push it with that.
If however the wood has fallen, and you want to administer a push: then introduce to it a concave instrument, so that its concavity enters into the tail of the arrow: then push it with that.
If however the arrowhead is barbed, then push it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is alleviated through that.
Which if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary, that the barbed flesh, in which is the poison, be cut out: if it is possible for you. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
Which if the arrow falls in the breast, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and is near to that which you touch with your finger, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting the vein, or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see, and treat it, until it is healed.
And this is the method of forceps, with which arrows are extracted.
Let there be similar extensions like a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp above the arrow, they do not release it.
And they are made of them in many forms, large and small and medium, according to the quantity of the size of the arrow, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its constriction.
The shape of a pushing barb.
And the shape of a pushing saw.
This is the extremity of the saw, like a radius, so that its entrance is alleviated into the barbed arrow, and it is pushed with it.
Of the cutting of veins.
The veins to be cut, the confluence of which has become customary in the body, are thirty.
Of these there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae; and two arteries which are visible on both temples; and two veins which are in the tear ducts of both eyes, called the Aspicientes; and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins which are to be cut for bloodletting in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of the veins of the arms is the Cephalica, and it is on the left side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, and it is placed on the right side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed above the elbow: and it is that in which bloodletting is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a visible appearance.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and there are two branches of it.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is the one which is under the curvature of the knee on the left side. And the second is the Saphena, and its location is at the heel on the inner side. And the third is the Vena Nesse, and its location is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
On the bloodletting of two veins behind the Ears.
Bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the Ears is a remedy for chronic catarrh, and hemicrania, and sahafati, and old bad head ulcers.
And the quality of bloodletting it is according to what I relate. And it is, that the head of the weak person should be rubbed: and his occiput should be frictioned at the place of the two veins with a rough cloth and good friction. Then the weak person should bind their neck with a ribbon, until the two veins appear. And the places of them are behind the Ears in two depressed areas of the head. Seek them therefore with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with a cautery.
Then take the lancet, and it is that which is called the Incisor. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the lancet reaches the mouth of the vein. Then raise your hand with the vein and the skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin by a cutting incision. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or around that, and make come out from blood as much as you wish.
Then bind it with poultices, and leave it, until it heals.
And now the incision of them and their cauterization has been mentioned at the beginning of the book.
Of the two arteries which are in the two temples the bloodletting is a remedy for old migraine and for chronic headache and for constant visual disturbance and for acute outflow of superfluities to the eyes.
And the quality of bloodletting them is according to what I relate to you. Let the weak person press his neck strongly, until two veins appear with a clear visible sensation: and the pulse of them is shown under your finger. Then indeed mark with cauterization.
Next raise the skin above the vein with your index finger, and insert the lancet from underneath, and raise the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did with the two other veins, and make the blood flow according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then release the tightening of the weak person, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards put a bandage and poultices on it, and tie it above with strong constriction: and leave it, until it is healed.
And now the remembrance of them has gone before, and their cutting, and their extraction at the beginning of the book.
The remedy of bloodletting the vein of the forehead is after the bloodletting of the head, for old facial pains: and for migraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its bloodletting is according to what I relate to you. Let the weak person tighten his neck with a bandage, until the vein appears. Next take the instrument that is called the Fossorium.
Place a prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorii above the vein itself, and strike it with a pectoral, or something else in its manner, and let blood flow according to the quantity that you want. Then loosen the weak perforation, and constrict it until it is healed.
And when it is opened with a broad lancet. Truly, it is not suitable that the lancet should be of acute extremity, as are the other lancets, but rather of broad extremity. And let the lancet be according to the transfusion, since the mouth is nearby, and perhaps the lancet will break when it is subtle.
The treatment of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, is for an eye with itch and redness, and dirt in the eye, and facial pains.
And the quality of bloodletting of them is, that the weak neck constricts with a thread. Then lancet them, and you should stand above its head.
And let the bloodletting be according to the transfusion for a small length, with a small lancet, broad for a short time, for there is no flesh in the location. For if the lancet is of subtle extremity, perhaps it will break.
Then cause the blood to flow as much as is necessary for you. And put a plaster on them, and constrict them for one night, and then loosen them.
The bloodletting of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent sneezing and facial pains, such as half-headache and redness which occurs on the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that it constricts the weak part of the neck. Then hold its nose with your left hand, and take a subtle and long bleeding instrument, and fix it in the middle of its lip, between that which distinguishes the nose according to straightness. Since a vein does not appear to the sense there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you withdraw your hand with the bleeding instrument for a little while, and let the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it on the same night. For it is restored quickly.
The instrument of Guidegi's bloodletting is for the constriction of asthma, and the interception of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and bad ulcers, and corroding, that is, itching eruptions.
And the quality of their bloodletting is that it constricts the weak part under both with a ligature on its neck, and let the artisan stand over the head of the weak one, and the weak one sits on a seat.
Then let the bloodletting instrument be applied to the length of the vein for a while according to the width of the bloodletting. Then extract from the blood a tempered quantity, or according to that which you see is necessary for it. Then do likewise with another vein.
Afterwards loosen the ligature, and constrict the two veins in the middle strictly: so that the weak one is not suffocated. And leave it until morning. For the wound will heal.
On the bloodletting of the veins according to Algeberich. The instrument of Algeberich's bloodletting of the veins is because it brings together to the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad ulcers: and cracks of the lips, and bad ulcers, also which are in the nose, and in its surroundings.
And the quality of bloodletting of the veins is that you should make the weak sit in front of you and tighten the neck with a band. Then uncover the lip of it, and look at the two veins: one of which is on the right of the lip, and the second on its left: and the indication of both is through the blackness of the gums. And that vein is because around the edges of them there are small, black veins. Cut therefore both with an oblique incision.
If however there is doubt about you, and you do not know which of them it is: then intend to cut the larger one, and the more obvious one.
And do similarly concerning the two veins which are in the upper lip: and mostly indeed habit is accustomed to it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Of bloodletting of the vein under the tongue, after the bloodletting of the head. The benefit of bloodletting of the two veins which are under the tongue is that the bloodletting of the head is for the obstructions which are in the throat, and for ulcers of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the quality of letting blood from them is that the weak should sit between your hands facing the sun: and lift up his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side of it one vein, and on its other side a vein: and the color of both is inclined to blackness.
Bloodlet therefore both: and beware, lest your last hand be in bloodletting them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps it happens that there is a flow of blood from those arteries.
The three veins which are bloodlet in the elbow, are those in which bloodletting has become a habit for all men.
And bloodletting of the ears is done in two ways. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle-shaped bloodletter, or with a razor to subtlety declined, or by cutting with a knife-shaped bloodletter, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and subtle kinds according to the abundance of veins and their constriction The form of the myrtle-shaped bloodletter is broad, as you see. And it suits for opening concave, full, protruding, visible, thick veins, and those which contain gross, turbid blood.
And this bloodletter is of lesser width, and of more subtle extremity.
And it suits for bloodletting of subtle veins, which contain subtle citrine blood.
However, the basilisk which is one of these three veins is for bloodletting a youthful remedy. For it extracts blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, because of that which follows the chest and stomach: and it suits the bloodletter to bloodlet with it, so that he may beware, and be above fear of it, for beneath it is an artery. If therefore he errs, and adds to the puncture of the bloodletter, he will wound that artery, and there will occur a flow of blood. Wherefore it is necessary, that the bloodletter does not bloodlet with a puncturing bloodletter: but rather cuts with an Alnesil.
For if the basilisk does not appear with a manifest appearance, then it is necessary that you displace it, and change to another, or that you seek some branch of it: or in place of bloodletting, a ligature of the arm. It is itself evident, and cut it with a scalpel called an escalaparius, as we have said.
But if you are going to perform venesection on the same, then it is necessary before tightening of the arm, that you touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark on the place with a cautery, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave section using a scalpel called Alnesil, as we have said. And let the incision not be deep so that the blow falls through the space to the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, an inflation in the place which you marked with a cautery, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: avoid it therefore.
For if you see blood at the venesection jumping out, like a child's urine, and it is subtle, red blood: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then press closely, and put your finger on it with a long pressure, afterwards remove your finger. If the blood is therefore cut off (for it is often cut off), then tighten the arm, and release it: and let the weak one beware of its motion: and let him be above its fear.
And do not move it for some days, until it is healed.
But if the blood does not stop, and overcomes you: and your medicine is not present at the moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For the extremities of it contract, and the blood is cut off.
Or take the cortex of a mat and bind it: take one middle piece, and tighten it over the place of the vein with a decent stricture, with a ligature and poultices until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with what preceded, recalling from the place powdered abscesses the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many conditions, because of the smallness of the wound's location, and the possibility of ligation from the arm.
The bloodletting of the dark vein is beneficial, which draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body: because it is composed of the Basilic branch and the Cephalic branch, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the bloodletter to be careful with it, so that he is above fear from its bloodletting, for beneath it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the puncture of the bloodletting, and finds the nerve, there occurs on it numbness, and the healing of it becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to sensation. If however it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and does not show itself: then it is necessary that you place your bloodletting where you will bloodlet with a cut with Alnesil, and divert the nerve with your strength.
For if the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in its length.
Of the Cephalic vein. The bloodletting of the Cephalic vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the head and helps against diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish, to bloodlet it by pricking with an olive or myrtle phlebotome: because it is a savior of all veins, because there is not beneath it an artery, nor a nerve.
Truly it is fitting for you at bloodletting, to divert with the bloodletting the head of the arm as much, and to seek a more tender place: and it does not harm it, if it is not found with the first prick, to repeat over it with bloodletting several times.
A true abscess sometimes forms in certain men when not bloodletting on the first percussion, but that abscess does not harm him in any way.
On the usefulness of Bloodletting, and precautions to be observed in it. The quality of Bloodletting, however, and its accidents from it, in whose correction you ought to consider.
First therefore it is of what you ought to know, that bloodletting is not administered, except in the conservation of health, and its preservation, and caution against the approach of illnesses.
And that bloodletting is performed on one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And that bloodletting is done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, and in summer, and autumn, and winter after digestion, when signs of repletion appear.
And bloodletting is done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses, does not have a determined hour: but when convenience and necessity demand it, at night, or day, and every hour, and every time.
And it is not fitting that children be bled, until they have fourteen years upon them. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth month to the seventh. Nor should the aged be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
When anyone wants to be bled for any reason: it is necessary, that he cleanse his intestines before bloodletting with a mild clyster, if there is much retained dung in them: so that the veins do not attract from the intestines during bloodletting putrid and harmful superfluities to the principal members.
Nor should blood be let from a patient who is suffering from nausea, that is, satiety; nor one who is drunken, nor one who is vomiting from intoxication, until that is removed from them. Nor the weak with dropsy.
And let bloodletting be performed again after a colic passion, and vomiting, and flux of the belly, and multiplication of coitus, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and everything that dissolves virtue from the bodily or animal.
Then consider in subtlety of humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible for him.
Then let a bath be taken, if nothing prohibits it: or let exercise be undertaken with some exercise, so that the blood may be made subtle.
And let bloodletting be applied to his chest, around the third day, as we have said: and take care, that his chest may be emptied on that day from all bad animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and punishment, and excessive coitus, and things similar.
And let him have present the things with which usage has created a habit in men from aromaticity, and fragrance, and rejoicing and similar things to them according to quantity of possibility.
Then let the bloodletter sit on a cushion, which is higher than the cushion of the one being bled: afterwards let him draw out his arm, and let the bloodletter rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then let him tie the bandage with a binding, and wrap it twice.
And let the binding be tempered, because the binding when it is unequal, or by excess in binding, then prohibits the flow of blood: or in loosening, then also prohibits the flow of blood.
