Then after tightening it is suitable that the hand of the bloodletter is rubbed together meanwhile until the veins become swollen and are revealed to the sense.
Then the bloodletter wipes the bloodletting instrument with a little oil, his own old one.
Then he places his index finger of the left hand on the vein beneath the place where he wants to perform the bloodletting, so that the vein does not flee and the puncture is made. For there is a vein which you do not find like a fleeing cord during bloodletting: and of them is one that is full of wind: and when you place the bloodletting instrument on it, it contracts beneath the instrument, and the bloodletter is deceived, and the instrument does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is constricted: therefore it is necessary that the bloodletter be steady, and be solicitous in all these things.
Then he puts down the bloodletting instrument. If then the vein is opened with one stroke, then it is good. And if not, then strike it another time in the same place, or above it with speed, if the location is not discolored.
What if it becomes discolored, or the skin becomes weak, then leave it for a day, or two days: and do not tie the bandage. Perhaps the warm discoloration will attract. Nor should he enter a bath.
Then let blood again, if it is loved.
What if the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly: and so that not more blood comes out than you want: then strike the bloodletting instrument again in the same opening, according to what is right, and add a little to the operation.
And do it with speed, before the location becomes discolored, for a discolored location is in many men during a small operation.
If then you see that it is already an aposteme, then you do not repeat anything on it, for it does not excuse anything with you. And put something on it from the dregs of oil, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your bloodletting, which you perform on veins to prohibit the flow of blood: and they make that the Tyrus and Alfarach, and Sagzenea, when it is put from one of them on the place.
For the blood is refined and resolved when it is thick.
What if it happens in the place of bloodletting a large aposteme, for it often happens, especially to him who is not accustomed to bloodletting unless by chance or is opening a small vein, then apply and put on the place a sponge soaked in hot water and salt, warmed a little: and bind it for one hour: because it will be resolved.
And it is necessary that you do that after the blood has come out from the same vein, when its flow is complete, or from another vein.
What if something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after days, it does not harm: and if you wish, put on it some aloe, or a solvent of myrrh: or something from the juice of mint, that is, calamentum or something similar.
And often indeed apostemes and swellings occur with bloodletting of the Basilica.
Therefore put your hand on it, then if you find softness upon compressing it, then that swelling is bad. Beware therefore, that you put on it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood will flow from the artery.
But make for him a plaster with that in which there is a styptic, so that the place may be induced. Then take care of it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from a man all the blood according to the quantity of his virtue. And that which appears from the color conquering over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out, until it becomes red: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it go out, until it becomes subtle: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs the extraction of blood twice, that phlebotomy of the vein be increased, and the phlebotomist be large: and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in the veins, and that the opening be narrow: and better what is administered in phlebotomy of the vein is, that it be a cross-section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the cutting of a nerve: and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom it is customary to faint at phlebotomy of the syncopis, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy a moderate bread infused in water of granated myrobalan: or secaniabin, if he is warm: and extract from him blood in three veins, or four.
And if he is of a frigid complexation, then let him receive before phlebotomy bread infused in syrup of myrrh, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in syrup aromatized with perfume.
If that should happen with syncope during phlebotomy, and is the cause of much blood exiting: then it is necessary to take in a drink of meat water, and subtle odoriferous syrup: and to use aromatics with galia, and to bind its chest with them: and to use the remainder which we said in the division on the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
Whoever wants to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time: it is necessary for him, for whom phlebotomy is on account of much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release the blood little by little according to the quantity of his strength over continuous days.
Who indeed wants to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do that after seven hours, or nine from his first phlebotomy.
Whoever wants to attract blood from his body to the opposite part to which it inclines: it is necessary that he be evacuated on the second day, or on the third.
In whose body there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes fever to happen: it is necessary that blood go out from it at once, and a large quantity be extracted from it: and the opening be widened until syncope happens: after which you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy: and that you put your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not happen in place of syncope. For it often happens, when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary when the use is to release the arm and release the blood a second time, since the opening of the vein has already been closed, and the exit of the blood becomes difficult, so that pressure be applied on it with vehemence, and the curved part be extended quickly. For that is a disease of the vein, or it diminishes itself, until it is bloodlessly cut with a knife.
Or remove it with the sharpness of a knife the blood clot that has formed in the mouth of the vein, and put on top of it something salty dissolved in water. And put on top of it some tyrical of alfaroch, or sassenea, and apply it with a gentle compression, until the blood comes out.

If however you are in summer days, then you should be excused from putting the hand again in warm water. And the flow of blood is put into warm water mostly in winter time.
And phlebotomy of this vein is beneficial for all veins, since there is not beneath it a pulsating vein, nor a nerve.
Phlebotomy of the right hand vein is beneficial for liver diseases.

Purges are made from hORNs, and from copper, and from glass.
And purging is done in two ways. With scarification, and extraction of blood: and the other is purging without scarification.
And this purging which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the purges which are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is the purging of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and the purges of Alhagdahim, and they are two sides of the neck on both sides together: and the purges of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower mouth: and the purges of the two shoulders: and the purges of Alachosos above the end of the tail: and the purges of the two elbows, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and the purges of the two thighs: and the purges of the two haunches.
And the venesection which does not extract blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that the virtue does not fall out from them, as is the case with it from bloodletting. And it is not necessary that venesection be administered with or without fire for any of the ailments which occur due to overfilling, until the whole body is evacuated.
What if necessity calls for venesection from an ailment, or because of habit, we administer it at every hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
What is it, for there is a man in whom when blood is multiplied in him to the point that he needs its extraction by venesection, he finds in his head heaviness and dizziness.
And there is one of them who finds overfilling and redness in his face, his head, and his neck. And there is one of them who finds itching in his face and his forehead and darkness and dimness in his eyes.
And there is one of them who scratches the place of his venesection. And there is one of them whose laughter is multiplied. And there is one of them who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or he spits up blood from his tongue, and spews out blood. And there is one of them whose sleep is multiplied. And there is one of them who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and wounds, and blows, and things that are similar to those.
When therefore we see something of those things, and it is specifically during the third quarter of the month: we prescribe then that venesection be done, after two or three hours have passed from the day.
The indication of venesection of the neck is: because it helps against heaviness in the head, and what flows down to the eyes. And it is necessary, that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this indeed is a substitute for the place of bloodletting on the head.
And it is not permitted that it be used by one who is of a cold complexion or in whom there is a catarrh. For it does great harm to him.

Of Venesection without scarification. Venesections without scarification are those that are applied to the liver, and spleen, and gout, and to the abdomen, navel, and kidney area, and armpit: because these members do not suffer scarification upon them.

For it rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain for that reason, because it resolves that venesection.
And it is also applied to the kidneys, when there occurs in them a stoppage or stone. For by the virtue of its attraction, it perhaps opens up the blockage, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And it does similarly when it is applied to the liver and spleen for a third venesection in both of them.

The form of a ventosa administered with fire.
The width of its opening is two fingers wide, according to what we have formed: and its quantity in depth is half a palm: and there is on its side in the place which is around the middle of it, a small hole, according to the quantity that an needle enters it: let it be made from bronze or citrine, with thick edges, smooth, equal, polished: so that it does not harm the member at its position above it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of bronze or iron, where a candle is placed with fire.
And now this large ventosa is made, larger than the one we described, and a little smaller than that one, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
For the ventoses of children, and of thin people are different, than the ventoses of men, and of those with large bodies.
The quality of placing this ventosa with fire on the member, is that you light a flax with fire from a linen thread, or a small candle made of tallow: and place it above the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the ventosa: so that the fire is lit around the lower part of the ventosa, so that the body of the infirm person does not get burned.
Then let it be placed on the member: and a finger is on the hole which I mentioned, until when we hold the ventosa: while we press, we remove the finger, and vapor will go out above that hole, and the ventosa will come loose at once.
Then apply a second dressing to it according to its size, and change it, if you need to.
In the case of a poultice that is administered for a sickness of the pleura with water, there is no rod across, nor an opening: and it is not administered unless to fill with water, and is placed on the member.
And this is its form.
And the larger this poultice is, so that it can hold a lot of water, the better it is.
Of the suspension of bloodletting.
Bloodletting is not administered to many, unless in those members in which it is not possible to apply poultices, because of their smallness, such as the lip and the gums, and similar things. Or in a member bare of flesh, such as the finger and the nose, and similar things.
And the quality of using them is: that they should be veins from bloodlettings which are in sweet waters, clean from rottenness.
Then they should be left for a day and night in sweet water, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their belly. Then the body should be treated first with bloodletting, and with poultices. Then wipe the weak member until it becomes red. Afterwards, something is placed on it.
When therefore they fill and fall, and it is possible to suck the place with a poultice, then it is almost the last in treatment: and if not, then wipe the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and rub, and squeeze out.
What if the flow of blood is prolonged after the operation of bloodletting, and it is that which flows back: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and put it on top, until the flowing back is stopped.
What if the blood is multiplied, then powderize over it astringent powders, or gall, and similar things from styptics: until the blood is wiped away.
Or let half roasted broad beans be placed over the place and left until they stick to the spot, for the blood is wiped away.
And it is necessary if iteration of bloodletting is required, when it is possible, to change them.
If the bloodlettings are prohibited from suspension, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood. Or apply a fig or a needle to the places, until something comes out of the blood. Then they should be placed, for as soon as they feel something from the blood, they stick at once.
When therefore you wish that they fall, powderize over them something from ground aloes, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall at once.
This chapter is also many times necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures, and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O sons, that the foolish medical men and the common people who have not inspected any ancient book, nor read a letter from it, throw themselves at this chapter.
And for this reason it happens that this method is deprived of the sciences in our land. And I have not found in it anything good at all, and I have not acquired from it that which I have acquired except because of the length of reading ancient books, and study over the understanding of them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
Then to adhere to experimentation and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from that in this Chapter all that my knowledge contains; and on which my experimentation was based, after I had shortened it for you, and freed it from the occupation of length, and shortened it by means of abbreviation, and showed it by means of declension: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declension, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is fitting that we say in the prologue of this Chapter, the sum of the discourse, and chapters, for whose understanding the artisan is first compelled to station himself upon their truth: namely so that you, and those who are prompt, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall happens to someone, then it is fitting that you should first hasten to phlebotomize him, and to solve it, or both at the same time: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of strength: or if he to whom it happened is a boy, or an old decrepit man: or if it is a time of vehement heat, or of strong cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold poultices, and flesh of birds, and of fish: and prohibit wine, and fat meats, and fill him with foods and every food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from the hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the member: then let the weak person return to his first regime with which his habit was accustomed.
When a fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the food be weak and nutritious, with much nourishment, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as food made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and innards of birds, and eggs, and fresh fish: and coarse wine, and similar things to these. For with this regimen the clotting of the fracture will occur more quickly and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, it is not possible that they knit and consolidate according to their natural ability always, on account of the dryness of their membranes: and they do consolidate and knit which are from them at the end of leniency, such as the bones of small boys.
However nature does attach to the fractured bone from every part something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, with which it adheres and binds itself, until they are knit together and bound together, until it comes to an end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until something does not hinder it from its operations: and on account of this cause it is necessary that you put weak nourishing food, in which there are heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of members: for the fracture of the thigh is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head: and the fracture of the bone of the chest is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the remaining members exist as diverse one from another: and I come with remembrance of every species of fracture exhibited in its distinct Chapter, separate from another. And the species of bone fractures are also differentiated among themselves.
Because its fracture becomes bent, besides which stars are affected in it. And when a fracture of it occurs along the length of the bone: and its fractures are jagged, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is adapted to a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and it is a fracture with a wound and a disruption in the head: and it is a fracture, a small split.
And every species has a device, or its own proper instrument for its restoration, according to which comes the recollection of it, distinguished in their own places.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is recognized, is its crookedness and its own projections: and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When therefore the crookedness does not appear in its place, nor the sound at your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the weak one find great pain: then there is no fracture there, yet it is possible that there be a crookedness or a small split: therefore it is not necessary to move it with the hand and compression entirely, but rather put on top of it from the medicines, whose recollection comes after the moment that suits the place: then let the dressing be tightly bound.
And know that the place when it is bent and split into two, besides which stars occur in it, nevertheless every part is moved from its comparison: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to the rectification of it, equaling it before a hot apostem occurs to it.
If however a hot apostem occurs to it, then leave it for some days, until the apostem subsides. Then equal it according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration of it, and its equalization, is easier than of a bone, in which stars occur.
Then tie it, secondly what will help you is the recollection of it.
If there are wounds in the bone, then extension of this broken member is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or foot: or with your hand, if the member is small: or with two cloths, or with a cloth and hand.
And let your position be where you put the member on an equal place, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try, that you make those additions return with all ingenuity, and in any manner you can in their places: and beware according to your strength, that pain or injury does not happen to the weak part through your operation: and try according to your strength, that you apply one of the two bones with its fellow, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time, that you feel and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the quantity of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many of the foolish do. For they often cause a warm aposteme or contraction in the member to happen through their operation: as we have already seen that from their operations many times.
Then connect afterwards equality and decent operation, and the tightening of that member, and rest, and tranquility: and warn the weak one about it. And make him beware, that he not move it during his hours of watching and his sleep, and his turning, and his agitation, and at his evacuation, and all his movements with his utmost care. And that he beware, lest the position of the member be a position which follows with pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the member's position: then it is necessary, that he changes it to another disposition from that with which he does not feel pain.
And let it heal with that, so that its position is equal and straight, so that no distortion occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the stricture of a fractured member is, according to which I will narrate.
Know that fractured members differ in their smallness and largeness, and their shape.
Which therefore of them are small, such as the arm, and finger, and elbow, and things similar to them, then it is fitting that there should be a wrapping of soft cloth, fine. Which however of them are thick, such as the hip and leg, it is fitting that there should be a wrapping on them of broad, hard cloth.
For a broad bandage connects a large member and binds it on every side with an equal constriction, so that it does not press into it a bruise.
Then begin after your reduction to put on a plaster suitable for it, in a gentle poultice over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap a bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the member deserves: and bind your hand for a little while with the bandage.
Then go with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and bind less than your constriction was, with which you bound the fractured place. Then stretch yourself with the wrapping from the place of the fracture for a little while, and gradually soften the constriction, until you take away from the sound place a large thing.
Then take another cloth, and wrap it again over the fractured place with wraps. Afterwards go with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in constricting and wrapping, and softening of it, according to what we said in the first wrapping above.
Then put among the dressings a sponge or a cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture will be straightened if there is crookedness in it and if not do not put anything on it.
Then dress over it another cloth. Afterwards put equal stars of plaster made to measure immediately over these dressings, if there is not in the place an inflammation, nor a hot aposteme.
If however there is in it an inflammation or an aposteme: then put over it what will appease that aposteme and take away the inflammation: and leave it for some days. Then bind over it then stars.
And let the stars be from halves of a reed cane shaved formed wisely: or let them be from wood from which are made cribs, which are made from pine or jerid, that is, branches of palm tree, or calamus: and let the width of all star be according to this form and the same figure.
Truly it is necessary that the star which is put over the dressing be thicker and wider for a little while than the other stars: and the length of the star be according to what is permitted by the member from its size and smallness.
Then bind over the stars with another cloth, according to the first dressing, the same. Then tie above with threads properly, according to what we have said about the dressing: and it is that your dressing be over the place of the fracture more: and as much as it is further from the fracture, the dressing will be smaller.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of stupid men who put their threads from cords of flax twisted, it is a great mistake: because the dressing falls with them beyond the quality.
And the thin threads do not meet again because you will not achieve with them that which is desired through tightening.
And it is not suitable for it to be less between the star and the star than a finger.
That which if a weak place is injured through the ends of the stars after tightening on healthy places: then put under them from lint or woolen cloth, until nothing is injured from it.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I come with the reminder of it in the second chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every broken member needs to be tied with stars from the first day. Which is, that when a member is large, it is not suitable to put stars on it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored, which are put on a fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common plaster abbreviated, restoring a fracture, suiting many complexions, especially for women and children: because it is from that on which neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is, that you take from the powder of a mill, and it is the marrow of flour which hangs on the walls of a mill at the movement of the millstone: and you mix it as it is, except that you sift it with the white of an egg: and you put its confection not thick, nor subtle. Then use it.
The method of another plaster, for restoring a fracture, and a dislocation, and a twisting. Take madder, and lavender, and acacia, and elecampane, and mugwort: and juice, of each a similar amount, myrrh and aloe of each a similar amount, and of the remainder twenty: and sift all, and mix with water of the remainder: or with the white of an egg, if the complexation of the weak person is heated. Then apply this plaster. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many men.
Another mode of plaster, which also benefits fractures and sprains. Take mugwort and mess, and white altea, of each a dram, myrrh and aloes of each half a dram, acacia six drams, Armenian bolus twenty drams, let all be rubbed down well, and crushed, and mixed with water or the whites of eggs: and administer.
A mode of plaster for joints, and bones displaced from their places: and it calms the accidental pain to them, and the cutting of bone, and the fracture. Let linen cloth be soaked and submerged in vinegar and decocted oil: and applied to the place.
And this plaster has not the virtue of restoration, but is good for soothing a hot abscess: and it properly expels pains.
A mode of plaster, which restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree and the leaves of the wild papaver: and rub all together, and make a plaster from both.
A mode of short plaster, administered for the restoration of a fractured bone: and when you wish to resolve the remainder of an abscess. Take the root of marsh marigold, and chamomile, and the flowers of violet, and the flour of the herb, all of one part: let all be rubbed and mixed with wine, if the member is not inflamed.
If the member is indeed inflamed, then mix it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and apply it.
A mode of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when there occurs a hard abscess during the restoration of a member. Recipe of the root of altea, and seed of linum, and balaustium, and mellilot, and samsuch, and flower of uiolarum, and camomilla, all alike, a single part, let all be mixed, and let them be made with water of willow, or with fresh water, or with wine. All that according to the warmth of the member, and the quietness of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a broken member for its restoration, except a cerate made from wax and oil: none other: and he described it as being a mean between thickness and fineness.
To Galen indeed it seemed, that things which have an exciting of heat should be placed on a broken member for its restoration, such as myrrh, and aloe, and olibanum, and similar to these from those which we described.
The method of an emplaster that is applied for a lesion and pain. Take mugath, and cicer, and cut human hair, or bird's wing, and altea, and salt in equal parts: let them be ground, and sifted, and made: and let an emplaster be made from it.
The quantity, however, according to which it is proper that the dressing remains, and then is dissolved, is such that you should consider at that time, if no pain happens to the infirm, nor itching, nor the broken bone moves from its place: then you do not dissolve it for many days. If it happens to him in the place a vehement itching, or an annoying pain, or inflammation: then dissolve it immediately, and remove the emplaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft marine sponge, humid: and submerge it in tepid water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is quieted, and the pain is assuaged.
If it happens to him in the place a vehement itching, or an annoying pain, or inflammation: then dissolve it immediately, and remove the emplaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft marine sponge, humid: and submerge it in tepid water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is quieted, and the pain is assuaged. Then leave the limb to rest for one hour. Then put on it a piece of cloth soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from the hot aposteme, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat the subtle dressing and a small plaster, and do not bind it with your first constriction, but bind it subtly, until it is healed.
Which if you see that the aposteme, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the accidents have been removed entirely, then repeat the plaster and dressing as you did at first equally.
Which if none of the things we have spoken of happen to the limb, then do not unravel it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, all that which appears to you from the solution of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of the flesh approaches over it: then also add to the dressing more than your entire first dressing: and also add in the thickening of the weak plaster, according to what the remembrance of it has preceded.
If however you see the place of the fracture already becoming dry and thinner than it should be: then know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Moisten it therefore with warm water at every place where you unravel it, and let it be for three days, and alleviate the dressing a little.
Through this operation therefore nourishment runs to the limb, and it is healed quickly.
What foolish restorers do from a fracture of the limb, instead of other things, when it is not restored at first as it ought to be, and is restored according to twisting, is an error of their operation and great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of them in all that, and it is better that it does not happen.
On the making of an accident in the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and its figures are many: and the causes of it are distinct.
For there is a fracture which occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone as far as it reaches to the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of that this species of fracture is named Axial.
Or the sword strikes a part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to the end of it: and this species of fracture is called Kalan, that is, Absolute Removal.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a crushing or an attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which approaches the periosteum, which is beneath the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and there is a wound of these two fractures also, either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone to the thinness of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and on account of that this species of fracture is called Capillary.
And there is a fracture which occurs from a fall or a blow of a stone, and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter inward, and a concavity is made in its place: just as it happens to cauldrons of copper, when a blow occurs to them, and a part of them enters inward.
And that often happens in the heads of humid bones, such as are the heads of children.
And there are for all these species of fractures separated dislocations, and I apply a vein with recollection of that entire thing in its place.
And we recognize all these species of fracture by detection on them, and inquiry of them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh that is on them.
The species of hairline fracture is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and wiped, and anointed on it with a plaster: for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, consider first the accidents of weakness: then if you see from the accidents what signifies a manifest sign of fear, such as vomiting of bile, and stretching, and loss of intellect, and cutting off of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these from accidents in many dispositions, without doubt it will not be cured: and if you see accidents not terrifying you, and you hope for health: then begin the cure of it.
What is, if the wounded comes to you immediately after being wounded: and it is in winter days, then it is necessary that you should strive for removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in summer days, then it is necessary that you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes corrupted what is under the bone from the pad, and those accidents occur which we have narrated.
What if the fracture of the bone has already come to the pannicle touching the brain, and it is with bruising, and attraction: then it is necessary that you should cut the part that is bruised and attracted, according to what I relate to you.
And it is, that you shave the head of the wounded: and you detect on the bone according to whatever mode is possible for you: and according to the figure of the wound, and what is least burdensome over the weak part.
What if it happens to you upon detection of a bone with a flow of blood, or a hot abscess: then apply to it what is appropriate. And it is that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and you make the wound rest, until the abscess has settled, and you are secure from a flow of blood. Then begin with the cure of the bone, and its removal.
And that will be done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is, that you cut the bone with a strict incising instrument: and this is its form.
Then administer another incising instrument wider than the previous one for a little while: and this is its form.
Then administer also another incising instrument even wider also than the second.
And the second method is, that you have a number of diverse incising instruments: some of which are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others: and let them be sharpened to the end of their extremities, and let them be made from iron or steel: and use facility in striking on the incising instrument, so that it does not strike the head, and wound it.
If the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary that you perforate around it, before you administer the incising instruments, with Awls which are called non-penetrating Awls: and they are not so called unless because they themselves do not penetrate to the end of the fleshy bone beyond what is behind it: on account of that an Awl has a round extremity above what is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents sinking and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to receive from these Awls a large number, each of which fits the quantity of thickness of the bone, until there is present to you an Awl for every cranium: whose sharp extremity in length and width is according to the quantity of thickness of that cranium.
And this is the shape of three types of trephines, or chisels.
The quality of perforating around the fractured bone is: that you put the trephine on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then change the trephine to another place and put what is around every hole the quantity of the thickness of the radius, or around it.
Then cut with the incisors what is around every hole in the bone: and make it thinner than you can on that side of fineness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand or with some other one of the instruments which you have prepared for it: such as small files and subtle forceps.
And it is necessary that you proceed with every caution, lest the trephine or incisor touch anything of the brain.
When you have gone around the bone perforating, and it has been separated from the brain, if it was continuous with it, and you extract it, it is necessary that you scrape, and equal the extremity of what remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. Truly, it is necessary that it be thin and more subtle than other incisors.
What remains of the small bones, and the fragments: then take them with ease, with that which is with you of instruments. Then cure the wound after that with bandages and ointments which we have said.
And the method of another operation is easy, far from fear: which Galen said, and praised it with great praise: and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to detect the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most vehement and most manifest: until when you have detected that part, and you have placed under it the extremity of this incisor, which has this shape.
And the lens-like part, nothing of the lens is cut away: and its pointed part on both sides presses in length, as you see: so that the lens-like part rests against the cataract, with its pointed surface pressing into the bone.
Then strike above the incisor on one side with a small hammer, until you cut through the whole bone with ease, as it becomes visible: and you will be safe from the cloth, which does not happen to it by chance, what you fear will happen.
And if the physician were not more stupid than men, and more ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the tip of the lens-like incisor, and would free it from it with ease. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and what is cut away from the bone is its surface, and remaining in it are roughness and subtle projections: then it is necessary that you scrape away that roughness, and remove those projections with subtle rasps which you have prepared in different quantities, so that it is possible for you to administer from them in every place the most suitable and straightest of them, according to what the operation leads you to, and the bone itself, broken, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your scraping, which you use to scrape the bone first, wider than those of the other scrapers. Then use after it a still finer one.
And it is necessary that you administer in your scraping, which you use to scrape the bone first, wider than those of the other scrapers. Then use after it a still finer one. And do not stop doing that according to continuity, until you reach administering the thinnest and subtlest of all.
In each of the remaining small fissures and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you administer care, according to what leads to its rectification.
And it is a thing which is not hidden from everyone, to whom in this art there is whatever study, and it stands upon that which we have written, and we show in great fractures.
If indeed the flap is detected and retained, when you extract the bone: then it is necessary, that you take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and put it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled, and submerge it in wine and rose oil, and put it over the first cloth: and make that as easily as you can over that, so that it does not press on the spleen.
Then administer over it a broad ligament: and do not bind it, except by the quantity that the wound holds so much: and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then undo it, and administer after that medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as the roots of lily, and flour of herb, and powder of frankincense, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is, that you make from these medicines a powder, which is powdered over the wound as are the dry.
And universally administer to it every medicine of the property of which is that it cleanses and does not bite. And take care according to your virtue, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it filth of ointment, nor foulness.
And take care according to your virtue, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it filth of ointment, nor foulness. And do not allow pus to gather in it at all: for when pus gathers on the brain's flap, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great evil befalls the weak after that: and now it befalls the spleen of the head, when the bone is cleaned by it, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider then, if weaknesses that we have mentioned befall, you should know what is harmful without doubt.
And if blackening does not occur, except from the medicine that is placed upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine, that it causes that blackening: then you should take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and bind them with a cloth. Then place it on the wound: afterwards treat it with species of cure that are suitable, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is fractured.
You should know that the nose does not break except in its two upper parts at the same time, or one of them: because it itself has two bones: since the lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break, and there happens to it only attrition, and twistedness, and curvature.
If one of its two parts breaks, then you should put a small finger into the nasal passage, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and this is done with skill. And beware, lest pain occurs during your operation on the weak area.
What if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it: then you should straighten it with the end of a rod, on which there is a small knob.
If indeed the fracture is in both parts, then do similarly to it: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, during the rest of a warm poultice.
Then insert into the end of the nose a bandage from linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the bandage a thickness of such quantity that it fills the nasal passage.
And certain of the ancient restorers said that they should pour the linctuses into butter and change them every day. That indeed does not seem to me, but rather it is necessary, that a linctus be poured into the whites of eggs, mixed with powder of toasted wheat. Then apply the linctus until the bone becomes firm and the cartilage becomes hardened.
And when a linctus is introduced into the nostril, a feather of a goose is placed in its stead, after which a soft cloth is wrapped over it: and this is done so that it may strongly hold the fracture of the nose, and so that the weak one is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not a necessary thing. If you want, do that: and if you want, make linctuses.
Which if it happens to the nose again while you are operating with a hot apostume, plaster the nose with cerate, or with a plaster infused in rose oil, or with a little of diacalendine.
If however the hot apostume does not happen, then it is necessary that you plaster it with flour of fava and powder of frankincense mixed with the whites of eggs. Then put a soft swab over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture, or are crushed, then it is necessary that you cut above them and extract them with instruments which are suited to them. Then sew up the wound, and cure it with that which brings flesh and consolidates from plasters suited to it, or from ointments.
Which if a wound happens to the nose, then it is necessary that you cure it with linctuses: and administer a pipe of lead, until it is healed.
Of the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken apart.
When the lower jaw is dislocated and its fracture is not with a wound, consider then if its fracture is only a defect and does not break into two, and curves inward, then its treatment is easy.
It is necessary therefore, if the fracture is in the right part, that you insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the weak person, similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand, and push with it the curvature of the fracture from within with ease to the exterior with your other hand from the interior part of the bone, and straighten it with that.
What if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from both parts, according to straightness, until its equality is possible.
If indeed a shaking or disruption has already occurred in the teeth, then tie them with a thread of gold, or silver, or silk. Then put a plaster on the fractured jaw. Then put over it a double-folded cloth, and put over the cloth a large strap made from the center, or a piece of leather sole, equal in length to the jaw.
Then tie it from above, according to what the dressing of it prepares for you, and its application is suitable, until it is not destroyed: and order the weak person to rest and quiet: and give its food gentle sips.
If indeed you think that something of the figure has already changed in some way, then hasten and release it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and apply a plaster made from flour of wheat, with the white of egg, or with flour of barley, after you have removed the plaster: and put over the plaster a gentle wool.
Therefore let that plaster remain over it and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not disturb it, until it heals: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is bound many times in three weeks.
What if meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its treatment, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider it. For now a fragment is separated from the bone, or by sawing: then be subtle in this, that you remove those fragments of sawing with what suits you in the removal of them from instruments.
If indeed the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then when you have removed those sawings, and none of them remain, or then the opening of the wound, if it is wide: and if not, put over it from plasters that suit for that, and flesh will grow on it, until it heals.
On the restoration of the Jaw, when it is fractured.
The Jaw is indeed fractured most often from the front part of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is broken and separated into two, besides which occur in that sawing: and it is easier to restore.
Or it occurs with a bone sawing: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without a wound, is that you make two attendants be present, so that one of them holds the aid that follows the fractured Jaw, and the other stretches the neck towards the opposite side.
Then you should reduce the fracture with your fingers until its shape is restored, according to what is suitable, and it should not be prominent or sunken.
What you squeeze in excess by extension, then it is suitable that you place under the bandage a sphere of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to the quantity of your need: and extend and elevate the splint, and bind the sphere with your hand, until it is equal to the fracture, according to what is suitable.
What if you cannot draw the extremity of the splint to the outside: because it penetrates to the depth: then it is suitable that the weak one should rest upon his neck: and you place under his shoulder a middle-sized pillow, and your servant presses his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the splint which is in the depth rises upwards: and then you straighten the fracture, and reduce it with your fingers.
If you feel a false movement from the splint, and it moves: then it is suitable that you bind above it, and extract the same false movement with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then make an incision of it with one of the incisors that you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed under the splint a tool that holds the flesh: and this tool is made of wood or of iron. And this is its shape.
It becomes similar to an ear which does not have a hollow.
And its width should be according to that which you judge suitable for it from the size of the bone and its thinness. Its length also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two extremities, as you see: and one extremity should be broad, and the other narrow.
If the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the false movement of the bone is broad, and you are concerned about a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or a hot abscess forms: then fill the wound with cotton pads, and poultices according to the quantity of the discharge of the wound.
What if a hot abscess forms, then soak a cloth in rose oil and vinegar and wine, and apply it over the top.
Inflammation of a bone when it is without a wound, and without a cut, is treated by applying to the bone a plaster made from milled flour with egg white. And put a linen dressing on top of it. Then apply a ball under the nipple, if you suspect it. Then take a hemiatem, that is, a rod which the Saracens hold, which should be long and of the width of one palm, or about that. Then put a double poultice on top of the dressing and plaster. Then take a little rod from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the rod in place of the hemiatem, which falls on the fracture and the location. Then put the hemiatem on the fracture as it is wrapped, and wrap it around the neck, and under the healthy nipple, and under the weak nipple: and bind it all over, and however you see that the bandage holds the fracture in place decently: and it is such that it should not be hidden from you, and altogether, so that the rod does not move off the broken bone. And for this reason, it is necessary to look at the weak side every day. As often as the bandage becomes loosened, and you see the rod already moving: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then put the bandage of the weak person on his back, and put under his armpit near his bandage a small pillow at night, so that with it the aid of it may be elevated from his side. The fracture of the shoulder is elevated therefore through elevation of the arm.
Or tie his arm to his neck: and do not untie the bandage, unless an accident occurs from itching, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see the surface of it towards that. And add to the tightening, and leave it, until it is restored and knit together.
And the fracture of the shoulder is tightened and comforted according to the most part in twenty days: and sometimes it happens in certain men in less than that.
On the restoration of the fracture of the Shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely broken, and in the wide place of it: and they are not broken from it except its edges.
When therefore the place is broken from it, and it breaks in the middle of it, and it is not recognized except by touch: then according to what is the figure of the fracture, prepare its preparation. And reduce it to its natural figure in every way of skill, that is possible for you.
Then put on the place powder of rose with egg white, and a soft cloth, and put over it a small pillow from folded cloth. Then put on it a broad strap from thin wood according to the quantity of the whole shoulder blade, or a little more than that.
If indeed under the strap there is a hollow in some place of the shoulder blade, then equal that hollow with a soft cloth, until the strap descends according to equality.
Then with the thread from above with a long needle and moderate constriction and firmness of the star, so that it is not removed from its place.
And consider the ligament in every direction. As often as the ligament is softened, tighten it with a thread, and equal to the star if it is removed from its place: and let the weak one lie on his healthy side.
And a spatula of some sort is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore the number is completed, then untie the ligament: and you will be in safety. For it is one of the bones whose fracture is not feared, nor their separation.
If a piece proceeds from the bone and pierces under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the furcula from the sediment of a hot aposteme, if anything happens from it.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the Breast.
The breast is rarely fractured in its middle, and that rarely happens to it. Its extremities are most often fractured and broken from its accidents.
When its middle is fractured, which declines to the lower parts, and there happens to it a severe pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps it vomits blood, and it is declared that there is a fracture in the bone, and it is not hidden from sensation, The restoration of it is, so that the weak one lies on his back: and put between his Spatulas a pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides or the sides with the hands: and adjust in its equality, according to every mode that is possible for it and is convenient, until the figure of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then put on top of it a plaster and a dressing, and put above it a splint made from a thin board of willow or hazel, and similar things for lightness, after you have wrapped it in a cloth. Then bind it tightly around the fractured bone, so that it is not moved: and take care with the bandage according to the roundness of the back, and tighten it with a suitable strap.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and as often as it becomes loose, tighten it: and if necessity demands its removal on account of itching, or corrosion which occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and remove it, and scrape away the plaster, and correct what happens with the method of its cure.
Then put back the plaster, if you see it is beneficial for that face: and apply a bandage to it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur, except in thick places which follow the back, and from the ends of them on the front side. And damage does not happen to them, except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from feeling on examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way, that you straighten the fracture with your fingers according to what is possible, until it takes on the shape, according to what is necessary. Then apply a plaster, and bind the fractured bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined to the inside: then there happens to the patient a severe pain and puncture, like the puncture that happens to one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also there happens to him difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And already the ancients have devised in it with many devices: and among them are those who say, that you should apply foods to the weak which generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly may be inflamed, and distended, and the fracture driven outward.
We indeed disapprove, that it not become a quick cause for making a hot abscess, if it does not happen. If however it has already happened, then he adds to it.
And some of them have said: let something be placed on the windy place, then let it be sucked with strength, and it is similar to reasoning. Truly however it is feared, that the windiness may attract superfluities to the place, on account of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them have said: it is fitting that the place be covered with wool which has already been soaked in hot oil: and little cotton balls shall be placed between the ribs, until it is full: so that there may be an even bandage when you wrap it around according to its roundness.
Then let the weak person be treated with a treatment for pleurisy from food and medicine.
That if the constraining thing harms the weak person, which it does not tolerate, and the mouth is pierced by a sharp pointed instrument, and we fear for the weak person: then it is necessary, that we cut above the place, and uncover the broken rib. Then let us put on it an instrument that preserves the health, whose reputation has preceded: and let us cut the mouth with ease, and let us cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and let us treat it with plasters, until it is healed.
That if a hot abscess happens meanwhile, then apply quickly, and pour in cotton balls soaked in rose oil, and put on the place: and let the weak person be treated with what is suitable for an abscess.
Meanwhile, it should also be bandaged over the side on which he sleeps, until it is healed.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
When a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens to them with a bruise, and similarly for the vertebrae of the back. When then this happens to someone, and you want to know, whether it will heal or not, consider this. For if you see his hands already having become soft and numb and dead, and he cannot move them or stretch them or clench them, and when you clench them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it and does not feel pain in them, then know that it will not heal, and in most cases it is mortal.
But if he moves them and feels the constriction with the fingers and the puncture in them, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe, and that the weak man will be healed with treatment.
But if it happens to the vertebrae of the back in a similar way, and you want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider his feet, then if you see that they are softened and it happens to them what happened to the hands, and when he lies on his back, gas and flatulence come out without his will, and when he is turned onto his stomach, vomiting comes out without his will, and when he is turned onto his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal. Do not then labor in its cure.
If indeed nothing of that has happened to him, the matter is easier.
And the treatment for what has happened to him is to prepare a warm poultice of the extract: so that you put on the affected vertebra rose oil alone, or with cooked egg yolks.
Place over it that same thing several times a day, until when the hot aposteme is seated: then place over the spot one of the comforting, drying, and constricting plasters, and tie it over with a ligature: and instruct the infirm to use quiet and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the side on which he feels pain, until it is healed.
What if it happens with the friction of a rough bone, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary to cut over the skin, and to remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnation, from ointments, until it is healed.
If the last part of the bone of the hyoid breaks, and is at the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, according to what is possible, and a secondary adjustment.
Then put over it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the Ankle.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the ankle are rarely fractured. What if they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not, unless it is a crushing in their extremities: and they split along the length, and bend inwardly: and there happens to the infirm a pain in the place, and a puncture: and the leg which is fractured becomes numb.
And the restoration of it is, that you extend your hand over it, until you stand on the fracture in whatever way is its figure.
What if the fracture is only in its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from the straightening, until it is made similar to its natural figure.
If the fracture is longitudinal, or it has already begun to displace inwardly: then make the weak part lie on its abdomen, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have made it even, put a plaster on it: then put on it a splint of wood or of leather: and bind it tightly with a bandage which you do not fear to move the fracture, nor remove the splint.
And make the concavity of those parts equal with what fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality: and order the weak person, to sleep on its back, or on its healthy side.
If a hot abscess happens to it: then cease from the extension of it, and its restoration, until the hot abscess has settled: and put on it what settles it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
If indeed an incision occurs in the fractured bone, or it becomes comminuted, extract.
On the restoration of the fracture of helpers.
A helper is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder blade.
If it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a wooden board of moderate thickness, according to the shape that follows.
And two bands are bound on the two extremities.
Then it is suspended from a raised place: and the weak person sits on a seat. Then he throws his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches to its elbow, on which the middle of the wood is attached the curvature.
Then suspend from above something heavy, so that it extends it downwards to the assistant. Then the doctor adjusts the fracture with his hands at the same time until the fracture returns to its place according to what is suitable.
And another method is that the weak one is placed on his own neck and his hand is suspended from his neck with a bandage. Then instruct two assistants, one of them to hold what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other to hold below it, and each of them to pull to his own side.
And if force is needed, so that the extension is stronger, then bind below the fracture with a bandage and above it with another bandage: and each of the two assistants pulls to his own two sides.
If the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed above that place and above the elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is adjusted according to what is necessary, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if not a hot abscess occurs.
If indeed a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the binding until the seventh day. And put on it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides: then bind it: and the method of binding it is that you put a plaster on the fracture.
Then put a dressing made from new cloth over the plaster. Then bring the arm up to the shoulder itself, and put his open hand on his own shoulder.
And put on a cloth, and bind it around the arm at the shoulder, so that the arm stands in its place, if nothing prevents you from doing so, nor is anything altered by you concerning the fractured bone.
What if something is altered on top of you from a broken bone, then use an astellis: and it is, that you put on the fracture itself an astellis which is wider and stronger than other astellis. And you put between the entire width of an astellis a finger's breadth, and let the length of the astellis be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers from each side.
Then bind over the astellis a bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your bandage be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture stretches, the tighter the bandage should be less.
If you see the astellis and the bandage as we have said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you apply a warm poultice, then remove the bandage and the astellis until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage for all three days, so that there does not occur at the spot itching, or swelling, or is prevented the reaching of food to the limb, on account of the constriction from the excess of the bandage. Correct the whole thing accordingly as we have said.
If you are above confidence, that nothing happens from it: then do not loosen the bandage, unless after three days, or more.
And let the weak person sleep on their back, and their hands on their stomach: and put under for support a mattress full of wool equally.
And consider every hour of the night or day, that the shape of the broken limb is not disarranged, nor the bandage loosened. Correct the whole thing with your virtue.
And give food to the weak person according to the order which we previously mentioned, so that it is subtle food at first, until the mouth begins to close: then it is necessary, that you thicken their food.
For it is customary for the aid of the thigh to be bound for forty days, then it is necessary that it be loosened and a bath administered and medicine applied with ointments which rectify it.
If the fracture is foul or bruised, then you should not loosen the bandage from it and keep it on for fifty days, and up to two months.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forked bones.
Of which one is small, and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is placed under the small one from the lower side.
Perhaps then the larger forked bone alone will break, or the smaller one, and perhaps they will both break together.
When therefore the upper small forked bone breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forked bone breaks, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones break at the same time.
If therefore the bone that breaks is the upper small forked bone, then it is fitting for the doctor in its restoration to apply a small extension with ease until it becomes equal.
If indeed the large forked bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to apply a stronger bandage.
And it is necessary to shape the hand for its restoration and to extend its extension on a pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers, and let the joint be lower than all the fingers or the rest, and let the weak one sit square on himself, and let the pillow be before him, or in his direction, in height, so that the weak one does not incur labor.
Then the assistant should extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another assistant should extend it similarly from above.
Then the physician should adjust the bone until he reduces it to the best shape that he can.
What if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then try to reduce every fragment into its place with your strength.
If indeed a separated splinter appears in it, and it punctures the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then sew over it, and remove it according to the method which we mentioned in the things that were previously said.
If the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter about that, so take the treatment from there.
What if a hot abscess happens at the beginning of your restoration, then cover it with a cloth made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be a medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a subtle bandage, until when the abscess settles: then remove the wax, and put on an prepared plaster made from powdered flour with egg whites. Then put on splints, and let the splint that is put over the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one forearm or in two forearms at the same time.
Then put your bandage over the place of the fracture stronger and more violent, and a little more with the bandage to the upper or lower parts of the fracture: or put on a softer bandage for a while, according to what was previously remembered at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be gentle cloths, moist: and not hard, rough. And let there be a thread by which it is tightened from linen properly, middle between subtle and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the member and ligament after days. What if something happens whose correction is necessary, such as an itch which happens to a member: then it is proper, that you bathe the member with warm water, until that itch quiets, and release the member not constricted, until it quiets, then repeat the constriction.
What if the constriction is already softened, and the wound already healed, and things similar to these: then correct that entirely with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prohibited from reaching the member, on account of the excess of constriction, then it is proper, that you soften it for a while, and release it for some days, until nourishment runs to it, then constrict it.
If however nothing happens to the weak one of those things which we have spoken, then it is not proper, that it be loosed, unless after twenty days, or around these.
Then let the hand of the weak one be suspended at its neck. And let its neck be equal, and let it be guarded by its strength from agitated movements. And make it sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
Of the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture rarely happens to the breast of the hand and the joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to them, unless from repeated pulling.
When therefore a fracture or pulling happens to the sole, then it is proper that the weak one sits squarely: and in front of him let there be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on the extended one.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician reduces them, until they are joined with a good joint.
Then it is suitable to apply a plaster and a dressing, if not a hot abscess occurs. Then place a bandage over it according to the size of the place, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is near the bottom around the sole of the foot, then make something like a sphere from cloth, and order the patient to press on it with his fractured sole, then it should be tightened with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the foot, and it should be tightened according to what is necessary.
If the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place a bandage over it, and another bandage underneath on the sole, and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a binding, as the hand turns, and extend between the toes with a ligature.
What if a fracture occurs to one of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be reduced according to what is necessary. Then let it be tightened with the sole, and if you love him, place a straight small bandage on it, so that the fracture may be elevated and not moved.
If the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then let it be reduced, and bound with the healthy finger that follows it, or let all be bound together in a row, and this is better. Or place a small bandage over it, as we have said regarding the thumb.
And consider your restoration, and after the arrangement of a hot abscess, and oppose with what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to which the account of it is repeated.
On the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone is often fractured and this is revealed by sensation, because it turns to the inner and back part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that one ligament is tied above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And a splint is applied over its surface.
Then every ministering ligament extends to its own part according to equality: and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the ligament be on the side of the chest, so that the extension falls upwards: and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is to the lower parts.
Then the physician makes the bone equal with both his hands, until he reduces it according to the similarity of its natural figure: and the bone is joined with a good conjunction.
Then it is fitting that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur on the limb. For if it happens that an abscess occurs on it: then leave it for some days, until the hot abscess settles, and then return to your treatment.
And the bandage of it should be thus. It is fitting that you wrap around the fracture a hard binding, that is, a bandage: and fold it twice or three times: and let excess remain from it. Then wrap the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper part of the two parts. Then scrape off what is excessive from the hip and the thigh from the aliment.
Then put on the hip in place of the fracture itself a plaster, and put among them one plaster upon the bone of the thigh.
Then fill the vacancies between the thigh and hip with soft cloths, so that it equals the bandage.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle above the place of the fracture, three or four turns. And the more you stretch with a bandage away from the place of the fracture, the less and softer and more gentle should be your bandage.
Then tie to both extremities a string, which you have inserted between the hip and thigh. Tie therefore with each of them three plasters that are above. Then tie with the two extremities of the string downwards until you reach the heel of the foot. And with each also tie the extremities of the plasters from the other part, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then leave the bandage upon it, while it remains, so that there does not occur to the limb corrosion, nor aposteme, nor inflammation, and similar things. What if anything of that should happen. Treat it therefore with a solution for it, and correct what has happened from that entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If indeed in the bone there is a fragment of bone which causes pain: then it is necessary that you should reduce it, if it is possible for you. And if not, bind over it, and remove that, and treat with what was mentioned previously, until it is healed.
And when this fracture of the hip is bandaged, besides what plasters are added to it, as we said for the shoulder and arm.
In the restoration of such a fracture no feeble limping occurs. And if it is restored alone, apart from applying the other leg to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is, always limps.
And know that the hip is constricted in fifty days, or a little added or diminished: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and the rest of the dispositions.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap of the knee.
Know that the kneecap of the knee rarely suffers a fracture, but it often suffers an injury.
