If indeed a fracture occurs to it, then it is not except for a crack or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing stands on that with feeling.
And its restoration is thus, that what has been disrupted from its parts is adjusted with the fingers, until it aggregates and is joined according to what is possible from equality and facility, and construction.
Then apply a plaster made from flour of fennel, or millet, with the white of an egg: and place on top of it a round bandage, if you desire that: and bind above a tight dressing fitting to it.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have narrated in the other fractures: such as a warm poultice and similar things: so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable to it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the fracture of the shin.
Know that the shins are two bones, of which one is thick and is named by the name shin: and the other subtle, and is named Fibula.
And it happens to both types of fracture what happened to the two bones of the arm: and because of that the restoration of it is made, just like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones are broken at the same time, the leg becomes crooked in all its parts.
And if a bone is broken finely, the leg becomes crooked to the front part.
And if a bone is broken thickly, you find it below: and it is one of those things which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and straightening, and binding of the stars according to that equally.
Truly it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having many sores: that the extension should be smaller, and your alleviation more gentle.
And in the leg there is something from the operation over the arm: and it is, when you equal the stars, and complete all your operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which is administered in the making of sandals, which is put under the planks: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which have small thickness, and are not from the smallest: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee down.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wraps according to its length. And put one part of the leg on one side, and the other part on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the bottom of the foot.
Then bind two splints in three places, at both extremes and the middle. For when binding is prohibited, the leg declines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now let a board of wood be administered according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be put on it, so that it may keep it from movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this for a fracture when there is a wound proper to it.
Then consider the leg every day, and examine it with utmost care for any abscess, or inflammation, or other remaining things. If therefore anything happens from these, then apply to it what is appropriate, until it is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture does not occur in the heel itself.
The bones of the foot sometimes suffer a fracture, and the toes also suffer a fracture to some extent; and this does not happen to them except through injury in many instances.
If a fracture happens to the bones of the foot and you see those bones being displaced on top of each other, then let the weak one place his foot on the ground prepared, as when walking.
Then stand and put your foot on what is elevated from those bones: afterwards press, or tread on it: and make it even, until it returns to its places.
Then put a plaster, and a dressing on top: and place under the sole of the foot a small plate, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with the foot a decent tightness, after which you wrap it with cloths and other things that it needs.
When therefore three days or four have passed for it, then untie the bandage. For you will find the bones even, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The operation therefore concerning these is as you see.
If some of the toes are broken, then restore them and make them even according to what I told you regarding the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then put a splint on the toe according to the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider than it: then place under the foot this plate that I told you about, and bind it with a decent tightness.
If indeed what is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then put a splint on each toe of its own quantity, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the plate under the foot: and let it stand on one end of the plate outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you do not forget, that you consider all that I have told you regarding other fractures from accidents that we have mentioned: and oppose each accident with what is appropriate.
Of the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the bone of the pubis, and the rod of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit cross-legged. Then lean her forward towards the same back part for a little while, and let it be held from behind.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with a cushion, until she fills it: and let it become in the vulva like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and her back be elevated little by little. For that cushion comes out, until it becomes before the entrance of the vulva like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns.
Then put a pillow on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cushion with ease, then she urinates: and reduce it according to the manner, in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her first pillow.
Let her do that for seven days, or around, for the bone is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a goat, and tie above its opening a reed canal, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the canal with force, until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with a cushion, and let her stand for some days, according to what we have narrated, until she is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or a woman is fractured, then administer in its restoration and its adjustment that which we have narrated regarding the bone of the ankle.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures which touch a little, especially to one who has something of study or usage.
And understand my book is this much, that the fracture itself signifies to you in many dispositions the right way for restoration of itself and its suture: understand therefore.
When a twig of a branch breaks, take the throat of a goose and insert the twig into it. Then put on it a dressing made from cloth, and it will be dressed, and let it be for three days or around that time, and it will heal, if God wills it.
On the restoration of fractures of bones, when there is a wound with it.
It is suitable for one to whom a fracture with a wound happens, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or the helper bone or similar to those, that you should perform bloodletting on him immediately: if the conditions of bloodletting are suitable for him, as we have mentioned before.
If the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you proceed to cutting it, so that you powder on it zeolite ground: if you do not have another thing at hand.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day: and do not postpone it, if there is no occurrence of an aposteme.
If a hot aposteme occurs, then leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the aposteme settles. And do not approach him on the third and fourth day at all, for you will cause bad accidents to happen to him.
If the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and equalize it with your hands with ease and a little extension.
If the extension of it and its equalization with your hands does not prepare it, then reduce it with this instrument. It is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And because of that it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four according to the quantity which he needs for that cure in every type of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be in it a small thickness, so that it is not doubled upon compression on it during the hour of operation: and let it be of sharp extremity, which has an edge at its extremities. And let its upper part be in thickness from the middle of it down to the lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the pointed extremity of it be placed curved over the extremity of the prominent bone: and drive it with that by one force, until when the bone returns and is equalized by a certain equalization.
Try therefore to make the extremities of the fracture equal to each other.
Which if the extremity of that fracture is subtle, and does not receive it with a good reception, then it is necessary to cut the extremity of that bone, until the instrument has power over it.
If indeed you cannot reduce them with what we have narrated altogether, then cut it with what is convenient for the incisions that we have said: or saw it with one of the saws in whatever mode is possible for you.
Then scrape what remains in the bone from roughness and small cortexes.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and after its reduction it finds a weak intense pain, then know that the bone did not return to its natural place.
Do that. For you confer on the weak one great help, when your restoration which you do for the bone is completed.
Then submerge the cloth in astringent vinegar, black. And this is especially so in summer. And do not apply a cerate over the wound, nor anything in which there is oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer asterions at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And split the detected wound: so that you cut with a fork into the openings of the bandages according to the quantity of the wound.
And beware with all caution, lest you constrict the wound with a fracture. For many foolish physicians have done this: and they have caused to happen to their weak patients either death or corruption or ichor, that is, sanies. And let your dressing be soft, gentle, a dressing diverse for the fractures and remaining things.
For if the wound is bad, or it is a great wound, and you find certain evil accidents upon it which we have narrated, and you find pain in the suspended place: then it is not suitable to apply asterions upon it. And make for them bandages of hard cloth in the place of asterions, and constrict it with these.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth which you placed upon it with wine. Then administer plasters and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as the four-dragon ointment, and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way, that pus runs out of it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass over the wound and it does not consolidate, nor is the pus separated from it: then you should know that there are small pieces of bone in that place. It is necessary that you examine the wound with a probe. Whatever of those small pieces of bone are separated, remove them, and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the membrane, and cause pain, try to cut around them, and remove them in any way that is possible for you.
If there happens to the wound an obstruction, or a swelling, that is, an ecchymosis, or another species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose each one of them with what suits it from the cure, whose narration preceded in its chapter.
And in that which it is necessary for my speech to stand, and to which you should turn your attention when a bone is broken, and protrudes beyond the membrane, such as the hip bone, and the shoulders, and similar ones of the large members, it is that you should not attempt its traction, for many times death occurs from that, but rather leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then cure the wound, if it seems to you, and if not, leave it alone.
On the cure of knotting in the healing of a fracture.
This knotting often occurs during the healing of a fracture. Especially, which is near the joints: and it forms from that a deformed figure of the member. And perhaps it prevents the member from its natural operation.
Consider then, if the knotting is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar ones.
Take from these some or all and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg whites, or with vinegar: and put them on the swelling in a poultice, and bind them tightly around it with a good bandage.
And tighten the binding, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is reduced.
Or bind over it a plate of lead that has been hammered thin. For lead has the property of removing all inflammation from the limbs.
If the swelling has already turned to stone and is constricted, and necessity urgently demands its removal: then make an incision above it on its upper part, and cut away the prominent excess: or shave it with certain scrapers, until it is removed: and treat it, until it is healed.
On the treatment when restoration occurs, and a member remains after that in a subtle state, not according to its own nature.
When a fracture of a bone is restored, and the member remains after that in a subtle, weak state: then this does not happen except for many reasons. Of which one is the multitude of the solution of the ligament and its ligation not according to what is proper: or on account of the excess tightening of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from running to the member: or on account of the multitude of excessive embrocation at an improper time: or on account of the scarcity of blood in the weak body, and its debility.
And the treatment for it is to feed the weak person, and to make his body tender, until the blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And bring joy and gladness to him.
Then put pitch on the member: so that it may attract much nourishment to itself. And let it be constantly moistened with warm water until nourishment grows and it returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored to their twisted state.
When a member that has already been restored after its healing becomes twisted and the bone which was fractured becomes prominent or knotted, and is deformed so that the shape of the member is spoiled, yet the member is not prevented from performing its natural operation: then it is not necessary to take the advice of one who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed greatly to be censured, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is as if it were mortal.
But if the lameness and knot are recent, then it is necessary to moisten with warm water, in which have been decocted softening herbs, such as the leaves of marshmallow, and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And let a plaster be made for it with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon plaster.
Or let the juice of the root of marshmallow be taken and mixed with the fat of a hen or iris oil, and plastered on with it.
Or let the fat figs be taken and pounded with dove's dung and other things that are named among medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with gentle, prolonged, and repeated rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And movement of the member should be administered to every part at all hours.
But if the twisting has already preceded and become strong and turned into a callus, and necessity to treat it with iron has been provoked: then it is necessary to cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what is in excess from the knot or bone be cut away with subtle incisions: and facility should be administered in that with solicitude and study.
On speech regarding dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any joint from its place. Because it is prevented from its motion, and the shape of the limb is deformed. And it causes pains to occur on the weak part, and vehement injuries.
When therefore a dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to the restoration of it, and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes calloused, and perhaps with that the reduction of the dislocation becomes more difficult.
On account of that, therefore it is not proper to move it, nor to extend it during the hour of its callous formation. Since it often causes spasms to occur, and painful injuries.
But when that occurs, then it is necessary that you hasten to the bloodletting of the weak part. Then let it be left until the callous formation subsides.
Then let the limb be anointed with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And let every limb be treated with that which will bring its memory back to its place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocations, and also according to what preceded concerning fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except in part.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two methods. For they are moved from their places by slight movements and made slightly soft. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they become soft towards the chest, until the saliva of the weak part runs, and it cannot hold it, nor can it close its mouth, and its tongue is involved with speech.
When however the dislocation of it is small, then it returns in many dispositions by itself with paucity.
If indeed the dislocation is complete and entire: then it is necessary that the reduction of it be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is, that the assistant holds the head of the weak person: and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between its mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts both his thumbs at the same time, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the rest of the fingers of his hands are outside, with which he balances.
And instruct the weak person, that they cleanse their jaws, and release them for chewing to every side. And the physician balances the jaw, and drives the jaw, until it returns to its place.
What if the reduction of it is difficult, especially if both jaws are at the same time: then administer a fumigation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor do you delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are balanced, and the mouth of the weak person is closed, and not loosened: then put over them little cotton pads, with cerate that is made from wax: and rose oil.
Then it is tied with ease with soft ligaments.
And let the weak person sleep on their back: and their head be steady between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left: nor is it forced, or does it force itself to chew anything: but instead a gentle enema is applied to their food, until when the pains are relieved, and the jaw is knotted: then let them eat what they want, and administer it with ease.
Let them not labor over the operation of their mouth with eating and drinking, and spitting, until the jaw is knotted and healed.
What if the reduction of two mandibles becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their place, then often fevers and constant sweating occur, and perhaps the weak belly is loosened, and perhaps pure bile is vomited.
When you see that, then know that it is a loss, and often he to whom that happens dies in ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the elbow and the extremity of the shoulder.
The elbow indeed is not dislocated from the inner side, on account of its continuity with the chest. And it is dislocated whenever to the outer sides, and that is made clear by sensation.
And its restoration is, that the weak person lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then bind the place with your palm, with a strong binding, for it will return by itself.
Then put on it a plaster, and poultices, and bind it.
The extremities which follow the elbow and continue with it are not dislocated, except rarely. What if it is dislocated for some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and adjusted according to what we have said, and what has been prepared for you.
Then put on it a plaster, and poultices, and a binding. And instruct the weak person to adhere to stillness and quiet, until he is healed.
And with this same cure, the extremity of the shoulder is reduced, when it is also separated from its place.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder is not dislocated, except in three ways.
One of them is such that it becomes displaced towards the lower part of the nipple. The second is such that it becomes displaced towards the part of the breast. And perhaps it becomes displaced to the upper part of the shoulder, and this happens rarely.
And it does not become displaced to the back, because of the blade. And it does not become displaced to the front, because of the nerve.
And most often it becomes displaced and protrudes downwards towards the nipple, especially in those whose flesh is scant. For it protrudes quickly in them and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely that it protrudes with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men a blow or a fall, and the shoulder becomes affected with a hot abscess, and it is thought on account of that, that it has already become displaced.
You should therefore try it, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its cure.
And the displacement is recognized when it is towards the lower part near the nipple, and you distinguish between a displaced shoulder and a healthy one. For you find between them a clear difference, and you find at the head of the shoulder a concavity, and under the nipple upon feeling the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg, and the affected person cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it becomes displaced towards the breast or upwards, then you find a palpable feeling, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this displacement is easy, when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the assistant lifts his hand to the upper part. Then put your thumbs under his eyelid, and lift the joint with strength upwards to its place: and let the servant lift his hand, and stretch it upwards. Then he should put it downwards, for it returns quickly by itself.
If however it does not return by what we have said, and there are many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the weak person should be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation which softens and soothes should be administered, such as if the roots of alteae, and foenugraecum, and mellilotum are decocted in water, and administered.
Then the weak person should be placed on his back: and a sphere and wool should be put under his eyelid, which is equal between softness and tightness.
Then the physician should put his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with strength, and pull the hand of the weak person downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you make the strong man present who is taller than the weak person, and stand at his side, and insert his shoulder under the eyelid of the weak person: and lift his eyelid upwards, until the weak person is suspended in the air. And another servant should pull the hand of the weak person to the lower part of his belly. If however the weak person is lightweight: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long stick be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like a mortar's handle: which is neither thick nor subtle. Then it should be placed under the weak part after they are placed on top of the head with wooden splints and bandages: and the weak part should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards from the other side, and his body should be extended from the other side with force: for the joint returns to its place with speed.
What if the reduction of it is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is to take a wooden piece whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a rounded head, so that its entrance may be alleviated into the deep wound, according to this form.
Then bandages of cloth should be tied over the rounded head, so that it does not harm the weak part.
Then it should be placed under the weak wound, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wooden piece to the lower parts: and the wood is tied on top with a bandage and arm and extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on the step of a ladder for width, and the hand extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. For the joint enters into place immediately.
When therefore its entrance is completed according to whichever mode is possible: then it should be placed under the wound a ball equal in quantity made of wool. Then place a plaster prepared from powder of milled material with oliban and albumen of egg on the whole shoulder, as it turns out above.
Then tie under the wound a bandage of suitable material: and roll a ligature over the plaster above: and attach his hand to its neck, and let it be, nor move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that food of little nourishment should be given to the weak person, until the member is comforted: for it is swift to its healing.
Then a larger dressing is applied after seven days, or five, and it is exercised with movement. If then it becomes firm and is not softened, then it is already healed.
If the joint becomes dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be administered to it, according to what was previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
What if all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and bandage are applied over it several times, and it does not become firm, and it falls, and it cannot be lifted to the upper parts: then know that the nerves which are at the head of the shoulder have already been cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
But a dislocation which occurs towards the breast and nipple, or to the back, is reduced with an impulse and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining bandage and care are administered to it, until it heals.
What if after healing heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the weak one use a bath many times, until that heaviness is alleviated, and it returns to its first nature.
On the cure of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: since it is evident to sight and touch, according to whatever figure it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, that is shown to you manifestly. For the joint is injured, and it cannot bend the arm, nor touch with it its shoulder.
And it is necessary that you should reduce the dislocation immediately, before there comes to it a hot aposteme.
For if a hot aposteme comes to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not be healed at all, especially when it is to the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and more vehement pain is to them: and many times death comes with it.
And the restoration of it, when it is among those things which are possible to reduce, is that the hand of it should be extended with two hands: and its arm should be stretched out, and the hands of the doctor should be above the elbow and below it, and he himself should push the joint with his thumbs or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he bends his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
What if the dislocation does not obey to reduction, then administer a strong and forceful extension: and it is that two ministers should extend the arm, and two ministers should also hold it, so that it does not move at the extension.
Then let the arm be turned to every part, after which a doubled and long cloth is wrapped over its hand, or a broad bandage.
And when the doctor touches with his hand the joint, let him anoint it with oil: that it may be among those things which help in the lubricity of the joint with facility. Then he pushes the joint with a strong push, until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that there be put upon it a plaster, in which there is styptic and excitation with the white of egg: and let it be bound with a moderate bandage. And let the arm hang from the neck of the weak person, and let it be for some days.
Then let it be undone. If then the joint is strengthened in its place, then undo the bandage from it, and let it be.
And if you see the joint not being well bound, then apply a plaster again, and a bandage, and let it be for some days, until it is bound. Then undo it.
If however a heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the arrangement is in motion: then administer a moistening in bathing, and subtle rubbing, and compression, until it is alleviated, or put on the joint a plaster of fat, that is, the grease of the spleen. Then let it be bound, and let it be upon it for a day and a night. Then remove it, and put it into a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with a moderate friction. Then repeat over it the application of ointment one time, and a second, and a third with the entrance into the bath, until it is alleviated.
And if you wish, put on it a cow's dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind over it.
Do that several times. It itself is alleviated, and returns to its own arrangement.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow of the hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, of the rest of the joints. Truly however it is necessary, that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes calloused: or else a warm callous forms on it.
And the reduction of dislocation in it is, that you put the weak hand of Mahassem on the table, and the assistant extends his hand; and the physician puts his palm on the prominence of the joint, and drives it, until it returns.
It is nevertheless necessary that it be considered, if the palm has already receded to the ventral side of the hand: then the weak one puts the back of his hand on the table at the extension and reduction: and if the dislocation proceeds to the dorsal side of the hand, then let the position of the hand be inward on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand be put on the table, so that the hand of the physician falls on the same prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster, having applied an apostema: and leave it, nor repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you over its reduction, after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its curvature: and nothing harms the weak one, except that the hand becomes soft, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are cut or bruised.
There is therefore no ingenuity in it, except that it be bound with a cauterization near its fracture. And perhaps it does not bind at all.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then put on it the plaster that we have described. Then let it be bound and left for five days: afterwards let it be unbound and exercised, and the hand moved.
But if its motion is hindered, and something heavy happens to it: then soften it with warm water and rubbing several times, until it is softened.
On the cure of dislocations of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated to any side, or a finger to the back of the palm, or its front: then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and hang it to the upper part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then hang it, and extend it, until it becomes straight on that day of its own.
When night arrives, bind it according to the narration itself: and do not cease to loosen it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it becomes tight.
And do similarly with it, if it is dislocated to the joint of the hand: and bind it to the same part, and perform your first operation with it, until it is healed.
And do likewise with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When there occurs to someone a dislocation of the vertebrae of the back, or a complete dislocation of the neck, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death quickly follows the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the bowel movement of the sick person goes out without control, and he cannot hold it: and often some of his limbs become paralyzed, or both thighs, or both arms of the same, or one of them: or one vertebra is displaced from its place.
For often it happens that there is a displacement, which often results in the displacement to four parts.
That which is displaced to the back is called Gibbosity.
The cure of it is, that you should consider then, if the gibbosity occurs from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing from it.
In that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to these, the ancients have already spoken of the methods of cure in lengthy discourse. Whose profit is not returned with advantage.
I indeed have already abbreviated from that, whose little is excused by much of it due to my proximity to the intention: and my exposition which I expounded on it, and formed its instrument in a different way than that which they showed it, and expounded it.
I say therefore, that in the humpness which happens on the front side, there is no ingenuity, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is done on the two sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which happens on the back properly, with what I relate: and it is, that the weak one is extended on his face on a level platform near the wall: and a wet cloth is spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then a straight stick is placed fixed in a hole in the ground, at the edge of the platform, around his head: and another stick around his feet at the other edge of the platform: and an assistant holds the stick, and it is not firmly set in the hole. And another assistant holds the other one.
Then tie over the head of the weak one, and under his armpits a soft bandage, firm: and extend the end of the bandage to the stick that is by his head: and tie it on that.
Then tie another bandage above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages, and tie them on the other stick that is by his feet.
Then every assistant extends to the part of the stick a bandage, and the two sticks are not removed from their places where they are fixed: nevertheless they are not made firm, as we have said.
And the physician puts his palms on the spine with force, until it returns: or he puts a board on it. Then he should tie his feet above the table, until he returns.
If however he does not return through this treatment, then take a tablet whose length is about three cubits. And let the place on the side of which we have spoken, which is near the weak part, be where the end of the tablet enters. Then put the middle of the tablet on the vertebra. And let the doctor place his feet on the other end. And let him pull firmly, until the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a turnkey that turns with your hand: and it is, as you fix in the ground by the head of the weak person at the end of the bed two sticks, whose length is one cubit: and by its head at the end of the bed two sticks.
And let the length that is between each stick be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place where the turnkey runs: and let each stick be firmly fixed in the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round stick be inserted: and it is a turnkey on which a rope turns in the two holes of the two sticks.
And on its end is a hole, in which the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm and that which turns: and in two other sticks there is similarly one of them.
Then pull the ropes that you have pulled on the chest of the weak person with the turnkey that is at his head: and the ropes that you have pulled on its legs with the turnkey that is at its feet.
Then a servant stands at each turnkey, who turns his hand with a rope, with which the turnkey turns: and the doctor adjusts the vertebra, according to what we have said.
This is the shape of the turn, which follows.
Then after the spondilis returns and the location is evened, then it is opportune to apply an excitatory plaster with egg white. Then a swab. Afterwards put on top of the plaster a bar from a board, the width of which is three fingers, or about that. And its length such quantity as to cover the place of the gibbosity and over the healthy part of the spondilis.
Then let it be bound with a bandage which we have mentioned. And let the weak person use subtle nourishment, until he is healed.
What if something of the swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing: then it is opportune to administer medicines which soften and soothe, with the administration of the bar which we have described for a long time. And now let it be applied on that plate of lead.
And when there occurs a swelling at the last of the spine of the back, and it is thought that there is a dislocation because of it, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Wherefore it is not opportune to prescribe for him with this medicine: perhaps indeed it will cause death.
Of the cure of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and the joints of the shoulder do not undergo anything except dislocation only: nor does there occur to them what occurs to the other joints from a slight removal and concussion.
And indeed the joint of the ankle is dislocated according to four parts. Which is, that it is dislocated to the front and to the back.
And mostly indeed it is dislocated to the inside: and a little is dislocated to the front and to the back.
And the treatment of its dislocation inward is that when you join the weak leg with the weak one, it is longer and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot bend its foot at the groin: and there is a place that follows the groin which is affected by a warm apostem, because the head of the hip has already reached there.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation outward happens is that they are accidents contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation forward happens is that it stretches the healthy leg according to its complement: nevertheless it does not bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot step on it forward: and its urine is retained, and its groin becomes affected by a collection of pus, and upon walking its foot is stepped on at the heel.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation backward happens is that it does not stretch the knee, nor can it bend before it bends the groin: and its leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is that you should consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic with the person having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its disposition: then there is no treatment for it at all. Therefore, it is not suitable that a complete reduction be attempted on it.
In the case, however, where the dislocation is recent and is one of the four types of dislocation: then it is proper to do this, that you should move the joint, and extend it inward and outward, and move it to the right and left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need other treatment.
If however it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you should prepare a strong assistant, and let him extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above his leg over the knee: and another assistant extends his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his armpits. Then he should tie it with a linen bandage over the hip joint, and hold the end of another bandage.
And let its extension be either from the front part of the groin to the region of the armpit, or from the back to the region of the spine.
And let the extension of all of them be in place of one, until the weak one is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this indeed is a type of extension which is a common type among four types.
If the displacement then returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every type to that which I shall speak of concerning its own cure.
In the reduction of it indeed, when the displacement is inward, it is necessary that the weak one be placed on his healthy side. Then a bandage should be placed over the hip joint, on what is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the region of the armpit.
Then another strong servant should take his arms, and hold the thick part of the weak hip, and pull it to the outside with a vigorous extension. For it has now returned to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of cure, by which this limb is reduced.
If however it projects above you, and does not submit to the entrance with this type of cure at all: then it is necessary that the two weak feet be bound together with a strong, linen bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees: and let the length of each be from its fellow as much as four fingers: and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the weak one should be suspended over the head of a wooden beam, which is in the house: and its length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
Then order a strong youth to support the hip so that it protects the head of the thigh, and let one weak youth suspend it, and let another youth push it, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In the reduction of it however to its proper place, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lies on a platform, according to what we have described for the one having a hump. And let a ligament be tied above his shin, weak in its proper place, and above his breast.
Then two sticks are placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then a stick is placed added in the middle of the platform, fixed well: on which are wrapped wet cloths, so that it does not harm the weak one: and let the stick be between his hips, so that it is not drawn to the lower parts at extension.
Then every assistant stretches to his own part, and the physician effects the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys to the reduction, it will be good.
And if not, put on it a board, and press on it, as we have said in the case of a hump, equally. Truly however it is necessary, that the weak one lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weak leg be extended quickly: and it is according to this same narration on the platform: and the physician puts the palm of his right hand on the weak groin. Then he tightens it with his other hand, and with that tightening he puts it extended downwards to the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the weak one be extended downwards, and let it be elevated above the ground: indeed it is necessary that it be placed on a hard object, as it is necessary, which is also done if his hip dislocates outward, according to what we have said about its placement on two hips: and it is on its face, and the ligaments are extended: according to what we have said above.
And it is necessary that compression with a pad be administered over the place from which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you stretch out the two legs of the weak one; and when you see them equal, and the weak one draws in its leg and stretches it out without effort: then you know that the limb has already returned according to what is necessary: then join the two hips, and put on a plaster, and tie with a bandage, that is, a wheel: so that it does not move to any part: and let the weak one adhere quietly for three days, or four.
Then untie the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is already stabilized: therefore let the weak one walk.
And if you see something relaxed: then reduce and plaster it, and tie it according to your first tightness: and leave it also for three days.
Then untie it and make it walk slowly on both for some days, until it is well comforted.
On the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways. It dislocates outward, inward, and downward, namely to the back.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you will make the weak one apply his leg to his hip. If it does not stick to the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is, that you make the weak one sit upright, so that he already stretches his leg if he can over it, and you stick your back to his face: and put his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere to your palms and straighten them between your fingers on his knee. Then apply your palm to both sides of his knee with force. And let another assistant stretch his foot until his knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction is that the leg adheres to the thigh gently, not with difficulty.
Then plaster it and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days or four. Then untie them.
And the weak person should not use walking for some days until he is healed.
If however the reduction of it is refused to you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose explanation has preceded in the cure of hernia, until it returns.
On the cure of dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone is sometimes moved by a small displacement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either to the inside or to the outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen and protruding on the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is easy reduction: and it is that it should be extended with ease by the hands and straightened until it returns.
In the cure of it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the weak person sit upright and a strong assistant hold him firmly from behind on the middle of his back.
Then hold with your right hand his foot from the upper part of it and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left two veins.
Then stretch with the left, and strike the chest of the foot with the right turned calf without effort.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then strike the chest of the foot to the leg in a third vein, and you will draw out the vein.
If then it returns in one vein, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot equalized, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it: for it will return.
If however the reduction of it is prohibited to you with what we have described: then make the weak one throw itself on its back on the ground, and fix a strong stake in the earth firmly, so that it falls between its thighs: and you have already wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not harm the weak one.
Then let the assistant take its thigh: afterwards the assistant will stretch the other foot, either with his hands, or with a bandage that is tied above the neck of the foot. Then let every assistant stretch against the stretching of its companion: and the stake stand between the two thighs of the weak one, retaining it, so that its body is not drawn to the lower parts at the stretching.
Then the physician equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy leg below. For the dislocation returns with swiftness.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and the health of its return is declared to you: then put on a plaster and a dressing, and bind it with strong bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or avoid the nerves which are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not pressing on them and harms them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. What if the ligament is softened, bind it tightly: then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and prohibit the weak from walking for forty days. If a wound is bothered to walk before that time, there will not be safety unless a dislocation is dissolved upon it, and it becomes rotten, and it does not receive a cure after that.
What if a hot aposteme happens to it: then it is necessary that you administer to its sedation what our narration previously mentioned in another place concerning the cure, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
On the dislocation of the toes and their treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them should be reduced with a little extension without labor: and that indeed is not difficult, indeed it is easy.
If the dislocation is in certain nodes of the foot's back: then it is necessary that the weak person make his foot sit on an equal place with the ground, or on a board: and he stands as one walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread on it with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you see that it is now equal, and the prominence does not appear in the place.
Then put under the arch of his foot a board that can hold the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind it tightly for three days with a suitable dressing. Then loosen it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured from the dislocation.
On the species of dislocations with a wound or fracture.
When something of that kind happens, and you consider its cure and restoration, death often follows: and because of this it is not necessary that one should presume to treat it, unless he who is prudent in the art through long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not daring, nor presumptuous: and that he use in apprehension medicines which calm hot apostems: and commit the weak to divine judgment. Unless you wait, or hope for salvation from destruction with gentleness of sickness, and in which appears something of the hope. I therefore consider reduction of it immediately at the beginning of the thing, before there occurs a warm aposteme.
If therefore the limb returns, according to which you wish, then administer the regime that calms the aposteme: and treat the wound with that which suits it from dried plasters.
What if the dislocation is with a fracture, and they occur in a separated bone of the breastplate, then consider removal of them, and do in that which we said in simple sicknesses from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your virtue, and guard yourself from entrance into the way of fear: according to what my preaching preceded to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more healthy to your body with them in your time.
